Section 1: Excellence 
Lignocellulose is the type of biomass with the highest availability in the world, being of parti cular importance for 
the bio-economy as the sustainable source of raw materials for the production of bio-based chemicals and materials 
as well as advanced biofuels. In Europe alone, lignocellulosic biomass has an estimated annual potential of technical 
availability of 1372 million tonnes, which could be sustainably used by 20303, doubling the current usage. However, 
in order to reach the goal of 25 % bio -based chemicals in 2030  (2015:14%), a major increase of the usage and 
processing efficiency of lignocellulosic biomass for sourcing the chemical industry would be required 4. Indeed, for 
the bio-based economy to reach its full potential in  its drive to substitute fossil -based fuels and chemicals by  their 
bio-based counterparts, it is imperative that non -food biomass including lignocellulosic materials are utilised  
efficiently. Currently, processing inefficiencies often result in relatively high concentrations of impurities and 
inhibitors, which often complicate conversion and  lead to resource-intensive downstream purification steps, thus 
resulting in low biorefinery yields in general  and poor product grade in many cases. Furthermore, the use of harsh 
process conditions entails high costs caused by energy, water and/or raw materials requirements; higher risk 
mitigation for process safety and complexity of operations and maintenance due to significant fouling, plant size, 
hazardous materials; waste and residues generation.  
 
These inefficiencies are particularly acute for the treatment of hemicelluloses  (HMC). Current lignocellulose 
biorefineries are indeed mostly focused on the conversion of cellulose into biofuels. While processes are well 
established to valorise two of the three major components in lignocellulose, i.e. cellulose (for fibres) and lignin (for 
energy), with significant efforts directed at dev eloping thermo-catalytic, enzymatic and fermentation processes to 
extract and convert sugars from the cellulosic and lignin constituents of lignocellulosic feedstocks 5,6, HMCs which 
typically account for 20-30 % (w/w), are often not efficiently segregated, purified, converted and transformed 
into useful and application-ready compounds, and thus are relatively under-exploited7. 
 
The source of the explanation lies in the complex 
composition of the HMC streams, which contain s 
mostly water-soluble compounds, which are readily 
mixable with the inorganic fraction of the biomass 
and the decomposition products generated during 
the pre-treatment steps. This, coupled with the high 
dilution ratios used, makes the valorisation of HMC 
challenging, resource -intensive, and thus 
expensive. Multi-step downstream separation 
processes are necessary to obtain the formed sugar 
streams in their purest form. Conventional recovery 
and purification of such sugars entails the use of 
high-temperature energy -intensive evaporation, 
distillation and crystallisation processes, with 
generation of high volumes of waste water and by-products. The use of harsh process conditions and the presence of 
species prone to decomposition can also lead to equipment fouling. All these factors contribute to product degradation 
through processing, and low yields of each of the components in the mixture . Therefore, conventional processes 
performance, material and energy efficiency, sustainability, safety and overall economic feasibility is often 
compromised. As a result, HMC streams are deemed difficult to valori se. Process Intensification (PI) has the 
potential to contribute significantly to the resolution of the challenges identified for the efficient processing of HMC 
by making biorefining processes faster at lower footprints , more efficient, more integrated and better for the 
environment. 
 
BioSPRINT aims at  utilising and valorising hemicelluloses, pursuing intensified processing steps and 
technologies towards ‘zero-waste’ bio-based operations and applying an integrated biorefinery concept that 
embodies the cascading principle  to create new HMC -derived products, so as to maximise conversion of the 
lignocellulosic biomass feedstock and its by -products, side streams and  residual streams into higher added -value 
products. The BioSPRINT philosophy is based on valorising the previously-discarded or costly side streams (thus 
‘closing the loop’).

1.1 Overall and specific project objectives 
1.1.1 Overall objective 
Following the PI concepts developed by researchers at the University of Newcastle (partner in BioSPRINT), in 
particular Kamelia Boodhoo 8, David Reay, Colin Ramshaw and Adam Harvey 9, and building on successful 
nationally-funded research projects ( Lignocellulose biorefinery and Lignoplast) as well as completed and ongoing 
EU-funded, H2020 and FP7 projects ( SelDES; ReInvent (BBI-IA-DEMO), IbD (SPIRE IA) , Carboprec (FP7), 
SteamBio (SPIRE, IA) and relevant assets, we will apply PI in the context of biorefining operations, so as to 
improve the efficiency of the  purification and conversion of sugars from the HMC fraction of lignocellulosic 
biomass and enable their transformation into new biorenewable resins for substituting fossil-derived polymers 
in a range of applications. The ultimate objective is to lead to a reduction in operation costs , feedstock and energy 
resources, GHG emissions and higher yields, while increasing operation safety by concentrating on PI technologies 
which can intensify biorefining processing methods and create an integrated biorefinery concept (Figure 1.1). 
BioSPRINT overall project objective: To develop, test and validate PI methods (up to TRL 4-5) for sustainable 
and cost-effective purification and conversion of sugars from the HMC fraction of lignocellulosic biomass to obtain 
biorenewable furan-based resins for novel polymeric applications. 
 
With regards to the targeted feedstock streams, of particular interest in BioSPRINT is the  realisation of our 
objective via application of the to -be-developed concepts and technologies for the exploitation and valorisation of 
HMC streams derived from hard wood and straw streams, from processes employed in the production of paper 
pulp or biofuels for example. Such streams are readily available from the pilot or large-scale production processes of 
research and industrial project partners respectively (Fraunhofer Center for Chemical -Biotechnological Processes 
(FhG) and UPM)  where they are usually not valorised  at all, or if they are, this is limited to biofuel and power 
generation uses. Additionally, a case study using HMC originating from Clariant, industrial partner associated to the 
project via participation in the project’s External Advisory Board (EAB) and provision of samples (see Letter of 
Support in Section 4),  will also be carried out. These existing streams have been specifically selected due to their 
industrial relevance, the opportunity they provide to work with different lignocellulosic feedstocks and processes, 
the variety of their characteristics, as illustrated in Table 1-1. Furthermore, working with industrial relevant streams 
allows us to validate the flexibility and modularity  of the BioSPRINT integrated and intensified biorefining 
technologies and processes. It also maximises the impact of the project, while facilitating the integration and scale 
up into existing processes. It will also greatly reduce the feedstock -to-waste and waste -to-energy flows in the 
biorefinery context.

With regards to processing technologies, BioSPRINT will focus on 4 activity areas (Figure 1.1): 1. Upstream 
purification 2. Catalytic conversion   3. Downstream purification   4. Polymerisation. 
In particular, BioSPRINT will investigate, develop and facilitate the integration and scale-up into existing processes, 
of integrated PI technologies for hemicellulose bio-refining operations. BioSPRINT will focus on:   
▪ An intensified and integrated purifi cation strategy  leveraging innovative anti -solvent precipitation and 
membrane separation methods in order to enhance the purification and concentration  of the C5 and C6 sugars , 
increase the selectivity of the process, minimise the amount of solvent used, and intensify the material and energy 
efficiency of the proposed processes. The membrane separations will be combined with spinning disc reactors (SDRs) 
for precise control over the precipitation reaction and properties of the particles formed. Water and solvent recovery 
steps will also be included for added-on sustainability.  
▪ Intensified dehydration of sugars to centralise the final products into furan -based monomers, in particular 5 -
HMF and furfural, making the most of all C5 and C6 sugars present whilst minimising further downstream 
purification needs. 
▪ Novel, intensified and integrated catalytic processes for dehydration of C5 and C6 HMC sugars into monomers 
for bio-based polymers. Development and formulation of heterogeneous catalysts based on super acid solids for 
sugar dehydration will be carried out via time-efficient, high throughput (HTP) screening methods in order to ensure 
maximum conversion and selectivity to functionalise the sugars towards the target furan-based products. Effort will 
be put into developing catalyst formulations that are tailored for the feedstocks in place, and integration with the 
solvent systems will be carefully designed, allowing simultaneous conversion of multiple sugar feeds.  
▪ Process intensification (PI) and recovery of reactive intermediate furans through extractive -reaction 
methods to isolate the reaction products from the reaction medium in situ. Cutting-edge Agitated Cell Reactors 
(ACRs) will be employed to intensify mass transfer rates and guarantee the yield of the extraction step. 
▪ Heterogeneous catalysts, intensified reactor and downstream purification design will be closely integrated to 
create a continuous processing cascade. The 
thermal unit integration of up -/downstream 
processing steps will be assessed in depth under 
operation. Liquid-liquid separation will be 
intensified using hydro cyclones and heat 
transfer through plate and compact heat 
exchangers, which will also minimise product 
degradation in distillation reboiling units.  
▪ Intensified p olymerisation of furan -based 
derivatives into novolac, resole and polyols will 
be conducted in a thin film spinning disc 
polymeriser capable of enhancing mixing and heat and mass transfer in the polymerizing film to produce a polymer 
resin with uniform properties such as chain length, whilst minimising further downstream purification needs. 
The BioSPRINT processing technologies will reach  TRL 5 for the majority of the technologies, and TRL 4 for the 
remaining ones, by the end of the project and then the overall improved biorefinery process will be validated up to 
TRL 5 via simulation using techniques such as Computational Flow Dynamics (CFD), process flow simulation and 
Process Analytical Technologies (PAT) solutions enhanced with machine-learning (ML) and virtual twins, etc.  
 
With regards to  new end-products, the intensified processes aim at purifying HMC streams and converting the 
sugar fractions into 5-HMF and furfural polymers for composite wood boards using resole and novolac-type resins 
and insulation polyurethane foams from polyols. The targeting of 5-HMF and furfural derived bio-based polymers 
and building blocks, obtained by conversion of C5 and C6 sugars by dehydration, seeks to enable the convergence 
of different feedstock molecules into similar monomers to allow maximisation of the final product yields. However, 
the concept can easily be extended and transposed into further sugar processing value streams.  The most promising 
products will then be selected and their synthesis replicated to optimise process conditions and assess average quality 
control (QC) properties, as well as their regulatory status (e.g. EU-REACH compliancy). Thus, BioSPRINT helps 
to create a new generation of bio -based materials and composites so  that higher-performance components can be 
produced and used in several industries. In  this way, BioSPRINT contributes to the desired increase in the market 
application of these materials. According to the latest market data compiled by European Bioplastics in cooperation 
with the research institute nova-Institute, global bioplastics production capacity is set to increase from around 2.11 
million tons in 2018 to approximately 2.62 million tons in 2023. For this to happen, it is vital to unlock new HMC-
based feedstocks and develop efficient and competitive refining processes, a vision that BioSPRINT fully supports. 
BioSPRINT will also promote an interdisciplinary and cross-sectorial approach to research and innovation: research 
and technology development will be complemented by cross-cutting activities including CFD modelling, process  
simulation, PAT, process optimisation using ML techniques, an integrated Life Cycle Sustainability Assessment 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.

(ILCSA), techno-economic assessment (TEA), standardisation activities as well as impactful dissemination and 
exploitation activities.

In BioSPRINT, in situ recovery of 5 -HMF and furfural as target products will be achieved 
through hybridisation of reaction and extraction  technologies. Aqueous dehydration of 
sugars using homogenous catalysts leads to low selectivity towards 5 -HMF and furfural, as 
these highly reactive intermediates can undergo further decomposition reactions, even during 
downstream purification steps. In WP2, selective heterogeneous catalysts will be developed in 
an integrated fashion with extractive-reaction technologies for in situ recovery of the products. 
Once captured in the organic phase, the target products will be stabilised. This will also drive 
any equilibrium reactions forward to accelerate these. Additionally, mass transfer to the organic

Furthermore, purification of the reactive intermediates to full isolation will also be intensified 
in WP3, p articularly for aqueous -organic phase separation and reboiling intensification in

decanting is slow and it will be accelerated using hydrocyclones, which will q uickly remove 
the organic phase from contact with aqueous phase and solid catalyst straight at the outlet of 
the reactor due to the compact nature of the units. Additionally, thermal decomposition during 
distillation operations will be prevented by developing plate reboiling units, which can operate 
with minimal temperature driving forces, while offering intensified heat exchange rates.

All requirements for RIAs detailed in the BBI Work Plan 2019 are being addressed: 
✓ BioSPRINT goes beyond SOTA by building on assets from previous projects listed on page 
19 of the proposal and further detailed in section 1.3.2.   BioSPRINT clearly demonstrates the 
benefits versus the SOTA both in terms of intensified processing technolog ies and product 
quality, including comparison with fossil-based counterpart resins. This demonstration will be 
further clarified by review of SOTA technologies which will inform the BioSPRINT 
technological agenda. The use of furfural and 5-HMF as building blocks will provide process-
related advantages. Being liquid at room temperature and characterised by low volatility, these 
can be easily added to the reaction mass through dropping funnels (on lab scale) or dedicated 
pipelines (on pilot and industrial sca le), without requiring dosing devices for solids (as it 
happens with paraformaldehyde). In this way, a more controlled and consistent concentration 
will be guaranteed. Moreover, 5 -HMF, being less toxic than formaldehyde, will show lower 
environmental and health risks during operations and ideally better labelling of the resulting 
products. Performance-wise, the unique chemical features of both furfural and 5 -HMF will 
contribute to the improvement of some important features of the target derivatives. In fact, their 
hetero-aromatic structure will increase the aromaticity of the resulting polyols, providing 
superior thermal and fire resistance with respect to formaldehyde-based ones. The replacement 
of the methylene bridge deriving from the use of formaldehyde/paraformaldehyde with an 
aromatic ring should help to improve mechanical properties (e.g. compression strength) when 
the final derivatives are formulated (e.g. rigid PU foams). 
✓BioSPRINT offers evidence, extracted from development up to TRL 3 which will be further 
up to TRL 4 -5, that the proposed biorefining technologies are more efficient and sustainable  
than current ones (see section 1.3.2 ‘Positioning of the BioSPRINT project on page 18).  
✓The BioSPRINT WP structure includes activities for value chain  stakeholder base building, 
BBI-JU engagement and liaison with other BBI and bioeconomy projects (ST7.1.3).  
✓ WP6 is dedicated to an Integrated Life Cycle Sustainability Assessment (ILCSA) according 
to ISO 14040/ISO 14044.  
✓BioSPRINT will undertake standardisation activities so as to establish a CWA on ‘PI for 
Biorefining Processes’ as well as participate in relevant standardisation technical committees. 
✓Compliance with relevant EU legislations will be addressed via a thorough assessment of 
safety challenges, including technical barriers as well as a rigorous assessment of product safety 
issues including regulatory aspects (e.g. REACH).  
✓ BioSPRINT also includes a techno-economic assessment (TEA) at T6.3.

1.3 Concept and methodology 
 
1.3.1 Overall concept 
The BioSPRINT concept is to successfully research the implementation and transposition of PI methods, which 
have proved highly promising or successful in other industries, to the purification and conversion of sugars from 
the hemicellulose (HMC) fraction of lignocellulosic biomass into furan monomers, and to the production of 
new biorenewable resins for several applications, including polymer applications from the obtained furans.  
 
In the context of conversion of HMC waste streams to bioresins, BioSPRINT will investigate the purification of the 
HMC streams to obtain pure C5 and C6 sugars through removal of lignin, inorganic species, small chain acids and 
other pre-treatment generated and introduced chemicals. This will be followed by the catalytic d ehydration of the 
multitude of C5 and C6 sugars towards furan -based monomers, in particular 5 -HMF and furfural to converge the 
range of target products and maximise yields. Finally, purification of the monomers and polymerisation into novel 
resole and novolac-type resins and Mannich-polyols will be addressed. Figure 1.2 serves to illustrate the BioSPRINT 
concept and the envisaged PI technologies at each of the four process stages. For simplicity and clarity, only the 
process units that will be subj ect of PI activities or new products are shown.  Additional ancillary equipment and 
secondary process units were not included in the diagram (e.g. solids removal by filtration was not included). 
 
 
Figure 1.2. BioSPRINT Biorefining Concept  
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  11 
In particular, the key operation units in the process pathway to convert HMC streams originated from lignocellulose 
fractioning pre-treatment is exposed, together with the associated inefficiencies in terms of CAPEX (i.e. large and 
expensive equipment), OPEX (i.e. low yields of products, large consumptions of chemicals, energy and large waste 
generation). The safety hazards are also highlighted. For comparison, the BioSPRINT intensified process route is 
shown too, highlighting reduction in CAPEX (i.e. small and cheaper equipment), OPEX (i.e. larger yields of products, 
reduced amounts of chemicals, energy and waste generation) and safety hazards. BioSPRINT will also make use of 
secondary processes and ancillary equipment, particularly biomass fractioning, solids handling and separation 
through standard filtration units, and hydrolysis of HMC into oligomers prior to upstream purification. However, as 
the aim of the project is to focus on the highest impact units for PI during HMC conversion, for simplicity and clarify 
of the proposal, such secondary and ancillary process steps, when performed through conventional technologies, will 
not be mentioned in this document as these will not be object of PI activities or other development work under the 
umbrella of BioSPRINT. T able 1-5 provides an overview of the envisaged technologies, industries (e.g. 
petrochemical, pharmaceutical , etc ) where they have been piloted/demonstrated for PI purposes, the potential 
identified for biorefining of HMC streams and the expected benefits from applying them in this context (e.g. lower 
costs, lower GHG emissions, etc.).

The following sections detail the concept of BioSPRINT in each of the focus technological areas. 
 
1.3.1.1 Upstream Purification of Hemicelluloses Streams 
 
In BioSPRINT, the key PI strategy will be the development of hybrid membrane technologies  as low-energy 
solutions to remove soluble non -sugar components and concurrently achieve higher concentration of 
hemicellulosic sugars. The membrane technologies can be combined with e.g. adsorption to ensure high purity of 
the target sugars. The aim is to increase the TRL level from 3 to 5. 
 
Two major challenges arise in the upstream purification phase: 1) separation of C5 and C6 sugars from inorganic 
species, small chain acids and other small molecules; 2) separation of sugars from extractives, lignin & non-volatiles. 
Membrane technologies (micro- (MF), ultra- (UF) and nanofiltration (NF), reverse osmosis (RO) and pervaporation 
 
17 https://www.amt.uk/application-technical-notes.  
18 (a) Boodhoo, K et al (2000) ., Appl.  Therm. Eng. 20(12), 1127 -1146 (b) Boodhoo, K. et al. (2003) ACS Symposium  Series No. 847, 
Washington DC (c) Boodhoo, K. et al. (2000) Green Chemistry, 2(5), 235-244 (d) Boodhoo et al. (2006) J. Appl. Poly. Sci., 101 (1), 8-19.  
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  13 
(PV)) combined with absorption will be studied in the removal of inorganic species, short chain acids and other non-
volatile impurities from C5 and C6 sugars from the d iluted aqueous and organosolv streams before catalytic 
conversion. In membrane technologies, the components can be fractioned by molecular weight, charge, diffusivity 
or vapour pressure. Therefore, the best separation result can be achieved by combining e.g. MF, UF, NF, RO and/or 
PV in series. To achieve high purity, membrane purification can also be operated in diafiltration mode. The membrane 
separation can also be combined with adsorption and photocatalysis or another advanced oxidation process to purify, 
recover and reuse both the process water and the valuable compounds included. A photocatalytic membrane unit 19 
developed at UOULU will be utili sed in the water recovery. Up to present, one technical challenge concerning the 
membrane separation technologies has been poor resistance of the majority of the membranes towards the prevailing 
operation conditions, such as extreme pH values, high temperatures or aggressive solvents20. Fouling of membranes 
and concentration polarisation can hinder their performance in both the short and long term. Hence, in BioSPRINT, 
close attention will be paid to factors affecting those phenomena. The composition of the feedstock streams will be 
analyzed in detail, thus enabling the selection of the most suitable membranes  and operation conditions for the 
purification. Prefiltration will be used to remove the solid particles that can cause mechanical abrasion or form a cake 
layer on the membrane surface. DoE techniques will be utili sed in selecting the optimal membrane materials an d 
properties as well as in optim isation of the operation conditions affecting the separation efficiencies and fluxes. 
Membranes coated with a photocatalytically active layer21 have been observed to reduce fouling. CFD will be applied 
in optimizing of the hydrodynamics of the processes further preventing fouling and concentration polarisation.   
 
Alongside the removal of impurities, membrane technologies may allow concentration of streams using considerably 
lower power-inputs in comparison to traditional techniques via replacement22,23 or hybridisation24,25. For instance, the 
intensified hybrid separation processes have been proved to reduce energy consumption of seawater desalination by 
14–39%26,27 and in alcohol or solvent dehydration more than 40%28. The intensified hybrid separation processes can 
reduce energy consumption of the upstream purification step at least by 30% 29 when compared with current 
distillation processes used to remove pre-treatment solvents and precipitate the lignin. In addition, some calculations 
show that hybridisation of the separation processes can reduce the total separation costs by 25 –60% compared to 
conventional processes 30.The membrane technologies also have a positive impact on operational safety as lower 
temperatures and decreased usage chemicals are preferred 31. The target of the upstream purification process is to 
obtain pure concentrated sugar fractions with at least 80 –90% recovery of the HMCs present in the waste stream 
representing an increase in total lignocellulose biomass recovery for valorisation of 20%. 
 
Additionally, PI methods for precipitation  will be used and coupling HMC hydrolysis with the separation 
processes will be investigated as strategy to aid recovery . The objective is to intensify the upstream purif ication 
stages by combining the unit processes into a (semi)-hybrid process demonstrating modularity of the technology.  
Additional PI methods for precipitation of lignin will be investigated in conjunction with membrane separation, 
particularly for the UPM stream where the lignin content can be high. Here we envisage applying solvent-antisolvent 
precipitation concepts to extract and precipitate the soluble lignin out of the aqueous phase. Particle uniformity and 
controlled rates of formation are essential to ensure product quality and easiness of solids recovery. To achieve 
uniformly sized particles from precipitation/crystall isation, controlling a uniform supersaturation level throughout 
the system is key. The Spinning Disc Reactor (SDR), by virtue of its rapid micro mixing in the high-shear thin film 
environment, has the capability to meet these requirements. By providing the opportunity for a homogeneous reaction 
environment in terms of concentration, temperature and mass transfer, the SDR will enable a tight control of 
precipitation rates and particle sizes, leading to high product purity. Several studies of precipitation processes in the 
SDR by Boodhoo’s research group at UNEW have indeed validated these expectations32. Therefore, BioSPRINT will 
build on that previous experience. By varying operating conditions in the SDR such as disc rotational speed, ratio of

1.3.1.2 Catalytic Conversion of Hemicellulose Sugars into Monomers 
In BioSPRINT, we will investigate, apply and integrate catalysis, kinetics and in situ separation methods of 
reactive intermediates into a single intensified process step to maximise sugar conversion, reduce generation of 
waste by-products, minimise hazardous solvent inventory and energy consumption, and improve process safety. 
The catalytic conversion of sugars will be developed via dehydration to 5-HMF and furfural, two platform molecules 
of high economical interest33. Several challenges are encountered in this process step, related to feedstock and process 
complexity, and instability of the reactive intermediates that constitute the target species of interest for downstream 
use34. Due to the existence of such challenges at feedstock at multiple process levels and scales, BioSPRINT ‘s 
intention is to address a number of inefficiency issues in this step using a holistic approach.  
In this area, the BioSPRINT work will focus on integrating into a single intensified process step three processes: (1) 
Catalysis, (2) Kinetics and (3) In-situ separation of reactive intermediates. Emphasis will be put into coupling 
conversion of multiple sugar species into a single stage process  by optimisation of reaction conditions, green 
solvent selection and use of ACRs as PI methods to intensify mixing and tightly control conversion and selectivity.  
Additionally, intensification via hybridisation of reaction and extrac tion techniques will also be investigated to 
enable in situ removal of highly reactive target products avoiding further degradation in the reactor. Integration 
between catalyst and reactor design will be achieved using HTP and ML methods for catalyst development, leading 
to more selective processes using less harsh process conditions. This will lead to an efficient reactor design, reducing 
reactor and downstream separations footprints. 
 
The first key challenge in catalytic convers ion of sugars from HMC streams is to address simultaneous conversion 
of multiple sugars undergoing chemical reactions at different kinetics. Due to the complexity of the mixtures which 
contain several different C5 and C6 sugars, there is the potential to generate a wide range of side products and tuning 
of catalyst selectively is compromised. The multitude of sugars present also leads to different conversion rates, and 
it is often necessary to extend the residence times in the reactors to allow sufficient conversion for slower reactions. 
Consequently, faster reactions can progress beyond optimal selectivity points, leading to degradation of the target 
molecules of interest. Current processes are scarce and mostly restricted to single sugar conversion (i.e. f ructose or 
glucose via fructose isomerisation). In BioSPRINT, we will address these specific challenges making use of HTP 
and ML methodologies to drive and accelerate catalyst development. HTP methodologies developed at UNEW35 will 
allows to create and test  catalyst libraries based in such materials in a short time period, whilst including activity, 
selectivity and stability enhancing elements such as add info here. Such techniques will allow the exploration of a 
wider range of process conditions and feedstock compositions than what is conventionally possible. ML algorithms 
and analytics prediction models developed by UOULU and eBOS, respectively, in the FUNBARK and CHARIOT 
projects, will be employed to create predictive ability between experimental data and accelerate catalyst optimisation. 
This will lead to fast identification of catalysts with maximum activity for conversion of complex sugar mixtures 
with optimal selectivity towards the key products of interest, with the objective being to obtain a catalyst wit h 85% 
selectivity towards 5-HMF and furfural, resulting to 20% reduction in by-products generation. 
In parallel, a detailed kinetic analysis employing multi-scale process modelling will be carried out by NIC.  
 
The kinetics of dehydration and by-products generation will be determined for the different sugars in the system 
using they key catalytic systems of interest. A kinetic model for the system will be established to allow determination 
of optimal operation points, paying particular attention to the coupling of reaction with separation as described below. 
Starting from idealised micro -kinetic models36, the objective shall be to translate the methodology to the realistic 
process operations through multi -scale mechanistic modelling developed at NIC37, improving the yield of 
functionalised bio-based furans (furfural, HMF, etc.) by 5–10% considering mineral acid-catalysed benchmark. First, 
the objective will be to proceed from unrecyclable mineral (Brønsted) acids, such as H 2SO4, which are applied 
presently, to solid catalyst materials, having a tuneable Brønsted/Lewis site number, acidity and distribution, which 
inherently also shifts the equilibrium of reactions. This will allow for higher furan yields, a more circular process 
(catalyst being recyclable), as well as PI facilitation (an easier acid maintenance in 1 phase only, e.g. by 
immobilisation). Secondly, the objective will relate to the application of the multi-scale process models to complex 
feedstock mixtures, which will allow for a quantitative operation description, especially considering the interactions 
of components, opening the door to optimisation. As an example, even upon applying solid acid catalysts, 
formic/levulinic acids as unwanted reaction products shift the  pH of media, thus inherently inducing a variation in 
catalysis as well. The third particular objective is related to the intensification by reactive extraction process.

Combining the latter with detailed mechanistic model, which will consider input feedst ock composition, 
thermodynamic equilibrium compositions, chemical reaction kinetics, mass transfer resistances and biphasic 
distribution coefficients will allow for optimising the yield of furans. This will not only improve the performance of 
reactive bio-refining operation, but will lessen the burden on downstream unit operations as well. 
 
A second key challenge in this process step is the reactive nature of 5-HMF and furfural in the dehydration pathway. 
Unless isolated in a timely fashion, 5-HMF and furfural can react further and degrade. This has led to development 
of expensive and complex processes that rely on the use of vast amounts of extraction solvents and which require 
large capital and operation costs to run multiple large extraction and distillat ion units to separate the products and 
recover the large amounts of solvent. Alternatively, current demonstration processes in place employ costly high 
pressure and high temperature hydrothermal routes 38. PI techniques play a crucial role in addressing this  second 
challenge in the system. In BioSPRINT we will exploit the use of extractive reaction processes 39 to isolate the 5 -
HMF and furfural reactive intermediates from the aqueous reaction medium. In this process, a solvent immiscible 
with water will be used to selectively extract the target reaction products from the aqueous phase, preventing further 
dehydration. Solvent selection will address the use of green solvents whenever possible, and it will focus on 
integration of in-situ extraction processes for multiple reactive intermediates into a single process, while optimising 
selection for rejection of the multiple sugars and by-products present in the aqueous medium. Solvent selection will 
be closely linked with activities at FhG to ensure easiness of solvent recovery and product purification downstream.  
In addition to PI through hybridisation methods to create an extractive-reaction process, the multiphase nature 
of this system leads itself to further intensification of combined solid-liquid reaction and liquid-liquid mass transfer 
rates through enhancement of the shear and mixing processes within a hybrid reactor-separator unit. In BioSPRINT, 
mixing intensification which is expected to enhance both reaction and subsequent extraction step will be tested using 
different technologies, including ACRs. 
In ACRs, the oscillatory nature of mixing disrupts established flow patterns, leading to intensification of mixing, heat 
and mass transfer. More specifically, the aim will be to increase liquid -liquid interfacial areas which will enhance 
mass transfer rates, allowing the reduction of the amount of extraction solvent used, resulting in small reaction vessels 
and lower separation costs downstream. 
 
1.3.1.3 Downstream Purification of Furan Monomers 
In downstream purification processes, a key BioSPRINT project focus will be the recovery of product(s), solvent(s) 
and unreacted sugar feedstock. Additionally, it might be necessary to remove short chain acids, humins and other 
unwanted by-products, generated in parallel dehydration reactions , to guarantee maximum product quality 
downstream. Similarly, to the upstream purification  units, emphasis will be directed towards integration and 
intensification of technolog ies capable of minimising net energy consumption and product degradation, 
greenhouse gas emissions and costs, associated with downstream separation processes. 
The following diagram (Figure 1-3) illustrates our concept for downstream purification of HMC streams: 
The development of downstream unit 
operations will revolve around producing 
different furan chemicals or mixtures (furfural, 
5-HMF and homologues) for the final product 
formulation by polymerisation, which shall 
ensue. The objective will be to attain the 
appropriate monomer purity  for poly -
condensation following. The reactor–
extractor unit upstream will provide the 
extraction of the furan molecules from the 
aqueous into the organic solvent medium in 
order to elevate the yield of products beyond 
single (water) phase thermodynamic 
equilibrium yield, whi le this extracting phase 
shall be the one to be treated downstream. 
A key challenge in this process section will be to achieve separation of the furans from the extraction solvent used 
and from other by-products formed upstream in a reliable and efficient way, while guaranteeing the required product 
purity. A continuous distillation process is anticipated to play a central separation role as this will provide the most 
efficient purification, in order to attain furfural, HMF or a mixture of derivatives, these being non-aqueous, as water 
would inhibit the kinetics/thermodynamics of polymerisation. 
 
Within BioSPRINT, downstream process operations will be developed, based on the bench scale determination of 
equilibria (Figure 1.4), which will be supported by quantitative structure–activity relationships (QSAR), both of

these playing a role in developing the use 
of pilot separation equipment  (Figure 
1.5) to deliver pure furan monomers. As 
the behaviour of the bio -based furan 
compounds during distillation, extraction 
or evaporation is not very well described to 
date, especially for  mixtures, the 
BioSPRINT approach shall also provide 
valuable thermodynamic data, as well as 
the equations of state, which will be 
translatable even beyond the BioSPRINT 
for emerging bio -refining downstream. 
Pilot scale campaigns, supported by 
simulations, shall be designed in a way, so 
as to examine the flexibility of the 
operation in terms of envisaged feedstock 
variability. As this is one of inherent bio-refining characteristics, dynamic process operation will also be examined, 
both in terms of simulations, as well as brief experimental campaigns, aiming at unchanged product quality. 
 
A second key challenge in this process section is to minimise both the amount of energy required and the degradation 
of reaction products during the high-temperature distillation processes. This will be achieved through a combination 
of energy integration and PI strategies. The implemented process integration of downstream shall consist of three 
main aspects, which are listed below:40 
• Thermal system integration (between distillation and other available streams, including reactive-extraction and 
pre-treatment streams available at UPM and FhG) will play a comparatively important role, as distilling will be 
energy-intensive; consequently, heat recuperation strategies will be duly implemented; 
• Merging unit operations by circumvented distillation and proceeding to polymerisation under the same solvent 
used for extractive-reaction; 
• Miniaturisation/modularisation/flexibility: the design of equipment will be revisited looking into parallelisation 
rather than scaling, whenever possible; distillation will be tested/operated dynamically. 
 
Various operation strategies (also linking pre -treatments upstream of the BioSPRINT process, reaction and 
polymerisation) shall be assessed in a multi -scale simulation manner.  Different unit operations will be linked in 
piping and instrumentation diagram (P&ID) models , analysis the effect of overall downstream integration, 
including energy integration with existing pre -treatment streams. Minimisation of the d egradation of react ion 
products during the downstream separation will be achieved using intensification strategies for water -organic 
separation and heat transfer intensification. Firstly, the separation of the immiscible water -organic phases upstream 
of the distillation proc esses will be achieved using hydrocyclones. In contracts with gravitational decanting units 
where long water-aqueous separation time would allow the reaction products to contact with the catalyst and aqueous 
phase, and hence undergo degradation, hydrocyclones make use of centrifugal force fields and are extremely compact 
units (centimetres in diameter) greatly enhancing the separation of phases with different densities. In this way, very 
short residence times (<1 min instead of hours) are attained avoiding the use of large footprint and slow decanting 
units in the process. Secondly, thermal degradation of reaction products in the distillation unit reboilers will be 
avoided by exploiting the use of plate heat exchangers and heat pipes. Plate heat exchangers m ake use of short 
diffusion distances and compact (but large) heat transfer areas to increase heat transfer rates, while making use of 
minimal driving forces, which allows precise temperature and over-heating control. Plate heat exchangers are much 
more efficient than traditional shell and tube reboiling units, typically having 80 -90% smaller hold -up volumes, 
which translate in smaller costs and hazardous inventories, 30% higher efficiency, better fouling resistance 41. Plate 
heat exchangers can operate with driving forces as low as 2 C, minimising the temperature of the contact surfaces 
and reducing product degra dation42. Also, heat pipes which act as passive devices where thermal conductivity is 
combined with latent heat transfer effects and isothermalisation, will be exploited. 
 
1.3.1.4 Polymerisation 
At the final polymerisation stage, resole and novolac-type resins and Mannich polyols will be synthesized based on 
the purified bio-based monomers produced , when necessary  in combinations with fossil reagents. Polymerisation 
intensification methods will be investigated so as to achieve continuous processes using spinning disc reactors (SDR).

In BioSPRINT, we will assess the usability of the HMC streams in wood laminates and panels and polyurethane rigid 
foams. In particular, the furan derivatives obtained from the catalytic conversion of sugars (e.g. furfural and 5-HMF) 
will be used as building blocks and functionalised to polymeric structures, as follows: 
a) Furfural and 5-HMF will be used in combination with petro-derived or bio-derived phenolics to develop novolac- 
or resole-type polymers; 
b) 5-HMF will be tested under Mannich-condensation conditions (in combination with phenol, guaiacol, cardanol, 
nonylphenol and alkanolamines like ethanolamine, diethanolamine, d i-isopropanolamine) to develop self-catalytic 
polyols suitable for 2K PU systems (e.g. rigid PU foams); 
c) The resulting furfural - and 5 -HMF-derivatives will be compared to similar products obtained from 
formaldehyde/Paraformaldehyde and used as fully-petro based benchmarks; 
d) The polyols thus obtained will be further functionali sed (e.g. by alkoxylation with EO, PO, ethylene carbonate, 
propylene carbonate or glycerin carbonate) recovering polymeric polyether polyols characteri sed by functionality, 
viscosity and reactivity thanks to the presence of different types of hydroxyl groups (phenoxy, primary, secondary). 
All the process conditions, raw materials selection and relative stoichiometric ratios, will be tuned and optimi sed 
during the project to guarantee the best yields and process efficiency as well as maximi sed bio-content. All the 
derivatives recovered will be characterised for their QC properties (e.g. viscosity, water content, acid value, hydroxyl 
value) and their synthetic processes replicated to define preliminary tentative specs. In addition to physico-chemical 
properties, average molecular weight distribution will be determined by GPC, in order to also define their regulatory 
status. In particular, EU-REACH prescriptions and polymer definition will be taken into account since the 
design of the molecular structure, therefore targeting new derivatives exempted from the registration process. 
Once characterised, above derivatives will be preliminary screened for their potential applicability as wood adhesives 
and in polyurethane applications in rigid PU foams, aiming at validating the technology at TRL5. Suitable 
commercial products (screened on the basis of their average QC properties and similar chemical backbone) will be 
selected as benchmarks, allowing a direct comparison to the possible advantages and limitations deriving from the 
use of furfural and 5 -HMF as polymer b uilding blocks. In particular, nonylphenol Mannich polyols, phenol - and 
cresol-based novolacs and resols, cardanol based novolacs and Mannich polyols will be selected and tested in 
reference formulations in parallel with the novel derivatives developed within the project. The aim of the comparison 
is not only to introduce a certain bio-content in the final polyurethane matrix, but also to overcome the status of the 
art by increasing thermal stability and fire performances, thanks to the introduction of the hetero-aromatic portion 
deriving from furfural and 5-HMF. 
 
With regards to intensifying the polymerisation processes for the above-listed products and derivatives, BioSPRINT 
will research the application of SDRs in view of achieving a continuous polymerisation process, which would 
replace the current batch-based approaches. 
 
The SDR technology 43, developed at UNEW and available at lab - 
and pilot -scale, will be evaluated for its ability to enhance the 
kinetics of the polymerisation to novolac-type resins and Mannich-
based condensation polymeri sation to polyols and to tailor the 
polymer properties to requirements. Centrifugal fields in a reactor 
may be generated by the rotation of the reactor surface or chamber 
whereby accelerations (=r.2) of the order of 100 g or 103 ms-2 may 
be achieved. Within such high acceleration environments, intense 
mixing and mass transfer capabilities have been reported44. The SDR 
has been shown to be a superior reactor for performing a range of 
polymerisation reactions including step-growth polymerisation45 and 
heterogeneous Lewis acid catalysed cationic polymerisations 46. 
The formation of furan -based polymers, particularly the polyols, 
investigated in this project is an equilibrium -controlled process 
which is driven by continuous removal of water molecules from 
the polymeri sation. Carrying out such a polymeri sation in a cont inuous thin, intensely mixed film on a rapidly 
spinning disk enables more efficient water removal and hence faster kinetics . Removal of water becomes 
especially challenging as the viscosity of the mixture increases and mass transfer rates slowdown in conventional 
agitated reactors. Here, we aim to maintain high water removal rates from the SDR film even at high conversions. A 
key aspect of the SDR technology is its ability to efficiently remove heat of reaction via the internal heat transfer 
system inherent in its design (as depicted in Figure 1. 6). This will enable uniform temperatures to be maintained 
within the processing film in order to safely conduct the polymerisation reaction.

1.3.2 Positioning of the project in relation to Technology Readiness Levels (TRLs) 
The project combines research and innovation activities for developing and evaluating PI steps and schemes for an 
integrated lignocellulosic biorefinery that processes HMC, so as to lower capital and operational costs for efficiently, 
sustainably and safely achieving high yields. Building on existing assets from previous projects, presented in Table 
1-6 below, BioSPRINT will transpose and validate the following new and improved process technologies reflecting 
a ‘TRL gain’, illustrated in Figure 1.7 below, from TRL 2-3 (project start) to TRL 4-5 (project’s end) as follows:  
▪ Precipitation process for hemicelluloses purification. It will be intensified by using technologies such as spinning 
disc precipitators for energy efficient and controlled precipitate formation, evolving from TRL 3 -4 to TRL 4 -5. 
Solvent-antisolvent precipitation in SDR has been demonstrated at lab scale in the  pharma processing industry for 
the recrystallisation of an Active Pharmaceutical Ingredient, curcumin and starch nanoparticles production. Applying 
similar principles, the TRL for the SDR precipitation of lignin will move from TRL 3 to 5 by the end of the  project 
when testing industrially relevant input streams 
▪ Membrane purification and concentration technologies for upstream purification of hemicellulose . 
BioSPRINT will investigate the precipitation  of sugars by purification and concentration of the HMC streams by 
membrane technologies, which will evolve from TRL 3 to TRL 5.  
▪ Solvent & water recovery processes. BioSPRINT will investigate water & solvent purification and reuse using  
membrane technologies, and solvent distillation processes currently in place  and photocatalysis (a photocatalytic 
membrane equipment47 developed at UOULU can be utili sed in simultaneous oxidation/precipitation and filtration 
of the impurities from the water). The TRL of the water reuse will be elevated from TRL 3 to 4-5.  
▪ Process for catalytic valorisation of sugars to furans . The catalytic conversion process will be intensified, 
continuous, scaled and integrated, evolving from TRL 3 to TRL 5 . Heterogeneous catalysts synthesis methods will 
be limited to currently commercially available technology for facile scalable fabrication and commercial ion 
exchange resins will be tested for comparison. Experience in operating intensified plant handling solids in the 
completed IbD project will be used.  
▪ Technologies for downstream purification processes will evolve from TRL 3-4 to TRL  5. The production of the 
bio-based furan chemicals from diluted mixed streams relates to downstream as well. BioSPRINT will intensify unit 
configuration, integrating them thermally, by merging, when possible, and by modularisation. 
▪ Polymerisation technologies . The most promising bio -based polymer formulations will be intensified in a 
continuous thin film flow spinning disc reactor which is already positioned at TRL 3-4 for polymerisation processes. 
With BioSPRINT, the development of the intensified SDR technology specifically applied to bio -based 
polymerisation will enable TRL 5 to be reached. 
▪ Upon the completion of the project, the ILCSA and exploitation activities will  confirm that the BioSPRINT 
processing technologies have the potential to be integrated into current industrial landscapes or existing plants, and 
thus within a 2-3 year horizon after the end of the project to be deployed and scaled up at large-scale to apply industry-
wide. For this reason, post -project opportunities will be identified to allow for the BioSPRINT processing 
technologies to be further evolved from TRL 5 to TRL 7 via  demonstration activities, and later TRL 9. The initial 
individual exploitation of the partners (Table 2-7) reflect the commitment to further progress the technologies on the 
TRL ladder. The economic performance check will also pave the way for the elaboration of a business plan. Section 
2.2.1.1.5 provides an overview of our market-oriented strategy for BioSPRINT.

1.3.4 Methodology 
 
1.3.4.1 Overall approach and 
methodology 
 
The approach of the BioSPRINT project 
(Figure 1.8) is based on the development and 
validation of innovative processing 
technologies for upstream purification, 
catalytic conversion / reaction, downstream 
purification of HMC and production of 
biorenewable resins.  Scientific activities 
cover detailed analysis of the industrial 
streams selected for validation as well as

and pilot-scale validation environments to reach TRL 4-5. Other scientific activities cover the legal, safety, 
standardisation part such as existing norms, laws or obstacles to overcome when thinking ahead of the project time. 
BioSPRINT overall approach will be grounded on the application of proven and mutually reinforcing  
methodologies for: a) Agile s cheduling; b) Feedstock s tream and further characteri sation and evaluation; 
c)Experiments in lab installations d) Definition of optimal process conditions using CFD, ML and predictive models; 
e) Lifecycle sustainability assessment; f) Multi-scale modelling and Techno -economic assessment; g) Compliance 
with existing regulations. 
 
a) Project scheduling 
The work flow in this project has been planned according to a model based on the agile methodology (parallel 
execution of WP 1 -4) with an important milestone being the completion of the lab tests of the BioSPRINT 
technologies before moving to the integration and validation (WP5)  of the technologies. As for WPs dedicated to 
project management, dissemination and communication, life cycle assessment, they crosscut through the project so 
that project’s results are continuously monitored, exploited and evaluated. The work plan therefore combines groups 
of parallel and consecutive WPs. DEC, as project coordinator, will ensure the execution of the project proceeds as 
per time plan. The WP structure (see section 3) ensures work is properly assigned, delegated and controlled to ensure 
on time, on-quality and in-scope delivery. W.r.t scheduling, the project will be executed over three contiguous and 
sequentially aligned phases (Figure 1.9). 
b) Detailed analysis of the feedstock compositions and variability  
WP1 will start with a detailed analysis of the composition of the feedstock streams that we will work with.  The 
analysis will be repeated before the technological integration for specific streams (WP5) start so as to cater for small 
variations encountered after the start of the project. This is necessary to ensure that a  co-creation approach with 
industry will be implemented and that the BioSPRINT PI technologies are developed with relevant, real -life 
applications in mind . In addition, i n biorefineries, the feedstock ’s natural variability throughout the seasons and 
available stock of tree species at different geographic coalitions means the process must be demonstrate flexibility to 
handle fluctuations in feed composition over time. Such disturbances can affect both the nature of the sugars presents 
and also how those sugars are bound to each other. The latter is influenced not only by the type of feedstock used but 
also by the lignocellulos ic biomass fractioning treatments. In such treatments, the HMC is solubilised but  often 
incompletely hydrolysed, and the streams often comprise a range of HMC oligomers displaying different degrees of 
polymerisation, with oligomers often including acetyl, formyl and uronic acid groups. The variability of the feedstock 
streams will ther efore be studied in detail. The detailed offline/online analysis of feedstocks will be achieved via 
different microscopic, spectroscopic, and chromatographic methods that are available . Fourier-transform infrared 
spectroscopy ((FT)IR) and Raman microscopy will be applied for potential solid residues. For liquid phase 
evaluation, gas chromatography (GC) will be applied for different volatile components, h igh-performance liquid 
chromatography (HPLC) for non-volatiles, such as sugars or oligomers, while gel permeation chromatography (GPC) 
will be used for polymers. Microscopy shall be applied to residue. 
 
c) Experiments in lab installations  
For each focus area, the experimental tests will be planned so as to demonstrate an outperformance of the BioSPRINT 
intensified processing technologies in comparison with the SOTA conventional technologies as detailed in section 
1.3 above. An experimental plan including given feedstock, challenges, and aimed results in terms of reference and 
target KPIs will be prepared. Iteration loops will be initiated and will finally lead to op timised system setups and 
operation strategies. This includes more specifically:  
Figure 1.9 BioSPRINT Project Phases 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.

• Analytical measured specification of heat and mass transfer resistances, reaction kinetics, losses and process 
energy requirement (different temperature and heat flow measurements, pressure, flow, etc.); 
• Definition of different process steps (which will in most extensive unit operation scenario be considered); 
• Reference benchmarking tests (applying standard feedstock composition or defined equipment operation); 
• Technological analyses, tests and campaign implementation (based on the evaluation of scenarios); 
• Test campaign evaluation considering the intensification of technologies (base case comparison); 
• Regular information exchange between process operation engineers, chemical development experts, etc. 
At a later stage, the BioSPRINT technologies will be evaluated and integrated specifically for the 3 streams studied 
so as to evaluate whether they perform well for each of the streams and are suitable for the conversion to polyols, 
novolacs and other resins of interest for the end-product producers of the consortium. 
 
d) Detailed definition of process conditions for each of the biorefining steps  (from upstream purification to 
polymerisation) using Process Systems Engineering (PSE) tools 
In alignment with the BIC SIRA, BioSPRINT considers that ‘ICT and related technologies can make producing and 
managing bio-resources more efficient, improve information exchange along the value chain, and by using advanced 
sensors, monitoring and control paradigms, can increase productivity and resource and energy efficiency’. Therefore, 
the experimentation part of the project will consist of the development and validation of technologies in 4 areas for 
which ideal process conditions will be defined and controlled using PSE tools and PAT as well as ML-based 
prediction models. Together, these initiate a rapid development of PI technologies and allow for better prediction 
of upscaling. CFD models will be developed in order to identify scale -up bottlenecks such as fouling tendency in 
intensified processing equipment, un even mixing, heat and concentration distributions, preferential flow patterns, 
concentration polarisation phenomena in membrane separation, and solids handling issues. The CFD models of the 
respective PI technologies with a validation process using experim ental results will lead a “virtual twin” of these 
technologies. PI may lead to situation where process control and monitoring encounter new challenges48. Therefore, 
an integrated process and control design approach is preferable to identify and solve the op erability bottlenecks 
arising in the development of new processes . UOULU, UNEW, ADSC , IRIS already have experience of applying 
such an integrated process design concept (IbD project 49). The sensitivity between the main operational process 
parameters, disturbances and critical quality parameters will be defined with Response Surface Methods from the 
laboratory results and CFD simulations, therefore providing a model-based approach for the process control, 
optimisation, and scale-up. Accordingly, the integrated approach will reveal the monitoring needs for the upstream, 
reaction, downstream and polymerisation processes, in particular related to the feedstock natural variability 
throughout the seasons and available stock of tree species at different geographic coalitions which means the process 
must be demonstrate flexibility to handle fluctuations in feed composition over time. The required instrumentation, 
in conjunction with the additional challenges by the PI technologies, will be evaluated and novel PAT solutions for 
the biorefining environment will be selected, adapted, and robustly employed . The monitoring approach will be 
complemented by advanced data visualisation and data analytics techniques contributed and developed by EBOS and 
IRIS, including data fusion strategies for maximising the information quality obtained from the different 
instrumentation and monitoring devices. ML techniques will be investigated for establishing a decision support 
system based on rule induction and case-based reasoning strategies for predictive maintenance and optim isation in 
terms of energy- and material efficiency. The advanced intelligence dashboard will be used for both understanding 
processes and for post data analytical purposes to assist in the refinement and improvements of their performance. 
This approach will warrantee: 1) Adaptability to the varying feedstock composition; 2) Standard isation of the 
outcome materials while optimising cycle time, as important parameter to support actual PI and  acceptance of the 
new monomers; 3) Verification of the actual removal of unwanted species . Finally, the process flow simulations 
carried out in WP5 will contribute to determine the plant-wide model (see point e below). 
 
e) Multi-scale modelling and Validation 
BioSPRINT is a project inspired by the need, highlighted by the BIC SRIA, to identify and use the right pathways 
for ‘making the best possible use of feedstock and energy resources, while linking all processing steps into an 
integrated biorefinery setting’. This is the reason why the validation of the BioSPRINT technologies to TRL 5 relies 
on modelling the entire integrated BioSPRINT biorefining concept and system from intake of feedstock and energy 
to output of products, taking into account the different integration and tailor-made paths for each specific feedstock 
– from feedstock intake and preparation, through the processing steps to the end product(s). The BioSPRINT models 
will simulate and assist in designing those pathways that wil l ensure the best possible utilisation of the various 
feedstocks, through the required chemical, physical and biotechnological processes to the desired products. MAT 
will be responsible for the development of these models using numerous sources such as lab oratory benchmarking 
of processes in terms of material and energy input/output, etc. The modelling will first focus on the current processes 
and then in the BioSPRINT integrated and intensified biorefining concept to enable performance, efficiency and 
sustainability comparison, leading to specification the ‘optimal use’ of the feedstock, in terms of the environmental, 
economic and social sustainability and the effects of processes and products.

BioSPRINT will then validate its intensified concept via the in tegration of PI methods into end -to-end biorefining 
implementation concepts (WP5) for 2 industrial streams from partners FhG and UPM. A detailed transferability case 
study for a stream provided by Clariant (EAB member)  will also be undertaken, in view of c onfirming the 
performance of the concept and its applicability / transferability / replicability to current and future industrial 
environments. 
 
f) Methodological performance assessment using Integrated Lifecycle Sustainability Assessment (ILCSA)  
A thorough ILCSA50 will consider effects of the PI methods selected in BioSPRINT along the HMC valorisation 
value chain and demonstrate the sustainability advantage across its technical, environmental, social and economic 
dimensions and hence increase adoption potential. The BioSPRINT ILCSA, taking into account the environmental, 
economic and social dimensions, will be carried out by IFEU using standard, certified and validated approaches.  
A dedicated analysis, led by UOULU, will look at improvements in process and product safety (decreased inventory 
of hazardous materials, less harsh process conditions, etc.). The ILCSA results will help the process developers to 
identify and quantify optimization potentials which improve the environmental performance of the produc ts. The 
ILCSA will also take into account the UNEP/SETAC 51 recommendations based on environmental and social LCA 
as well as LCC, since it is essential to use precise analysis of the impacts to back up the decrease in GHG emissions; 
increase in resource and energy efficiency and decrease in CAPEX and OPEX. The LCA  methodology will follow 
ISO 14040 and 14044 covering the entire life cycle of the involved products using a three -step approach: (1) Goal 
and scope of the specific system for the different validation cases will be defined in order to enable a scientific 
comparison of different technology alternatives; (2) Detailed life cycle inventory data on energy consumption, 
material use, and emissions, will be gathered as foreground data from the project partners using partner -specific 
questionnaires as well as from literature. The life cycle inventory models will be set up in a dedicated LCA software 
and connected to background inventory data from the global Ecoinvent v3 database14. The respective indicators will 
be calculated, such as the contribution to climate change by the emission of GHG based on the IPCC52 global warming 
potentials with a time horizon of 100 years, non -renewable primary energy demand53, and a set of further midpoint 
indicators that cover a range of resource categories and emissions such as eutrophi cation, acidification and 
stratospheric ozone depletion. In addition, newly devised impact assessment methods for capturing phosphate 
resource depletion, occupation of natural land and a new methodology (aLUC, attributive land use change) to 
overcome limitations of assessing indirect land use change (iLUC) will be used; (3) Based on a contribution analysis, 
environmental hotspots will be identified and positive impacts from reducing GHG and resource consumption will 
be quantified. The results will demonstrate the sustainability advantages of using the BioSPRINT technologies. Data 
from the pilots will be extrapolated to allow an assessment at industrial scale, thus estimating the overall sustainability 
potential of BioSPRINT for the European economy.  
 
g) Techno-economic assessment (TEA) 
The multi -scale modelling will also enable a detailed techno-economic assessment (TEA) of the integrated 
BioSPRINT biorefining concept, for which MAT will also be responsible. A TEA method will be used for an early 
concept evaluation at the beginning of the project. It will estimate the production costs of the process concept from 
the raw materials of the process to the final products (biorenewable resins). The results of the ‘light’ TEA will support 
the experimental research by identifying the key economic parameters and factors. A more detailed TEA, carried out 
at the end of the project, w ill result in a production cost estimate for the main product, which includes annuali sed 
capital cost estimate. Production cost will be divided to variable and fixed operation costs and capital costs. Variable 
costs and revenue estimates will be calculated  on the basis of operation and performance data. Fixed costs are 
estimated by using factors available in the literature and also based on project partners’ expertise. Investment costs 
of equipment are obtained from the project partners. Total investment cost will be estimated using factorial method 
for cost estimation. Suitable economic indicators may also be used to estimate the feasibility, like return on 
investment (ROI). Sensitivity of the production cost to the main cost components will be analysed. 
The TEA will include an economic viability performance check of the processes developed, including value chain 
and market analysis to assess the economic viability of the processes and explore cases for replicating the use of the 
PI approach for sugars biore fining and beyond. In addition, these efforts will guide the positioning of the bioresin 
products in later commercialisation stages. Such studies will also allow comparison with current benchmarks (target 
is to reduce costs and biomass losses by at least 10%). The industrial feasibility, as well as the cost competitiveness 
for the newly developed polymers will be investigated.

The effective validation of novel processing technologies for intensification of HMC biorefining operations requires, 
throughout the project , to consider the wider applicable regulatory landscape. An overview of the most relevant 
regulations, mostly European directives,  which will have to be taken into account , together with their national 
transpositions, is provided below. A thorough review will be undertaken as part of WP6 (T6.5 dedicated to process 
and product safety, led by UOULU) so as to derive process and product regulatory requirements. 
 
Directive 1999/13/EC ‘REACH’. BioSPRINT extraction & purification processes will lead to the production of two 
substances, furfural and 5 -HMF molecules, which have a full registration (10.000 – 100.000 MT/year)  and a  
registration for “intermediate use only” respectively for REACH. On that basis, BioSPRINT will aim at g enerating 
derivatives that are exempted from registration, i.e. that are “polymers” according to the definition of ECHA . AEP 
and PFR  will undertake periodical reviews of the REACH status (exemption), of the “polymers” end-products as 
part of T6.5. 
Directive 2008/50/EC regarding concentrations of major air pollutants; It sets legally binding limits for 
concentrations in outdoor air of major air pollutants that impact public health such as particulate matter (PM10 and 
PM2.5) and nitrogen dioxide (NO2), possibly encountered in BioSPRINT. 
Directive 2010/75/EU on industrial emissions (IED). It aims at achieving a high level of protection of human health 
and the envi ronment taken by reducing harmful industrial emissions across the EU, in particular through better 
application of Best Available Techniques. It is applicable to biorefining operations. 
Directive 2012/27/EU on energy efficiency. It sets targets for energy savings obligation in end use and incudes rules 
on metering and billing of thermal energy; on monitoring efficiency levels in new energy generation capacities. It is 
relevant to BioSPRINT which aims at achieving energy efficiency gains. 
ATEX Directive 2014/ 34/EU related to potentially explosive atmospheres . It covers equipment and protective 
systems intended for use in potentially explosive atmospheres and defines the essential health and safety requirements 
and conformity assessment procedures, to be applied before products are placed on the EU market. It is relevant as 
it is applicable to the environments in which the BioSPRINT concept will be tested and validated. 
Directive 2006/42/EC on machinery; It promotes high level of protection for EU workers and citizens when using 
machinery. Operational, occupational, process and product safety are relevant to BioSPRINT scope and work. 
Other: Regulation (EC) 1272/2008 on classification, labelling and packaging (CLP) of substances and 
mixtures; Local legislation & regulatory frameworks

for biorefining operations have not 
featured strongly in the vast standards 
literature. However, examining the

standardisation developed as part of the IbD project, the consortium aims at making a strong contribution to the 
elaboration of such standards with: the establishment of CEN Workshop Agreement (CWA) for the establishment of 
a working group dedicated to ‘PI for Biorefining Processes’ and (b) contribution of DEC, UNEW, UPM, PFR and 
AEP in Technical Committees dealing with Product standards, Installation standards, Recycling standards as well as 
Handling & Transport standards (e.g. CEN/TC 383 CEN T/C 114; CEN/TC 462; CEN T/C 193). The consortium’s 
standardisation activities (Figure 1.10) will be coordinated by DEC as part of T7.4.  
 
1.3.5 Interdisciplinary Approaches and Stakeholder Knowledge 
The BioSPRINT project involves strong inter-disciplinary considerations, as it involves various natural (physics, 
chemistry and biology), social (business, communication, economics), as well as applied sciences (chemical,  
electrical and mechanical engineering branches). These are interwoven  within the BioSPRINT activities.  A broad 
range of interdisciplinary skills are present in the consortium, including : ✓PI expertise ✓Innovation engineering 
expertise ✓ILCSA expertise ✓PSE and PAT expertise ✓ML, data analytics, visualisation and data fusion  
✓CFD, computer -aided simulation expertise  ✓Expertise on market analysis, economics, techno -economic 
assessment.  
✓Expertise on industry-related business, quality, legal, gender and ethical considerations.  
✓Expertise from socio-economic and legal disciplines (e.g. education, sociology).  
✓Expertise on commercialisation, business management, marketing of innovation in the industrial sector. 
Together, the partners have the skills to develop the required biorefining technologies, integrate and validate the 
integrated concept up to TRL 4-5, and exploit the results of the project. The consortium has the relevant knowhow 
(as evidenced in Section 3, Figure 3.4) to carry out the interdisciplinary aspects of the project, integrating knowledge 
from also external stakeholders via the EAB.
