1.3.6 Gender Aspects, Sex & Gender Analysis 
We are convinced that ‘an in-depth understanding of men and women's needs, behaviours and attitudes can improve 
the scientific quality and societal relevance of the produced knowledge, technology and/or innovation’  (EC 
Vademecum on Gender Equality ). As such, it also leads to enable innovation in products, processes  and services 
better suited to pot ential markets, an aspect particularly important for BioSPRINT, which is geared towards a 
commercialisation trajectory. At proposal stage, given that the object of the project is not directly related to persons, 
no special gender dimensions have been identified with regards to technical development. However, our innovation 
and the project will consider the gender dimension in relation to our capacity-building, D&C & exploitation activities 
and jobs creation. G ender will be considered in the training & education materials and the target groups of the 
Training, Education and Capacity-Building programme (WP7) as well as in the exploitation strategy of the project 
(WP8). Regarding gender balance issues as part of the consortium, specific information is provided in section 3.3.
1.4 Ambition 
1.4.1 Progress beyond the state-of-the art (SOTA) and innovation potential 
The overall ambition of BioSPRINT is to establish its integrated biorefining process from hemicellulose (HMC) 
to biorenewable resins as the technological, economic, environmental and social reference for the valorisation 
of the HMC fraction of lignocellulosic biomass , allowing transition from batch to continuous processing, 
coupling of previously distinct processes , as well as generating important efficiency gains  through process 
intensification (PI) and new products.  
Through the transposition and implementation of advanced, novel and integrated PI methods  to biorefineries, 
covering the entire span of the HMC biorefining process , from upstream to downstream till the final 
polymerisation stage, BioSPRINT aims at creating promising interconne ctions in the value chain and at opening 
opportunities for business, income and jobs in the bio-based economy.  
 
Through the extensive literature and practices review from international sources, scientific articles, publications, 
projects and initiatives, it is evident that that only limited attempts at the valorisation operations of the HMC fraction 
of lignocellulosic biomas s were identified, with PI being most often overlooked . It should be noted that the great 
majority of the identified relevant activities, projects, approaches and initiatives in the context of lignocellulosic 
biorefineries were focusing in cellulose conver sion to biofuels, with more recent developments including lignin 
valorisation, leaving the HMC fraction untouched or only used for energy generation . This represented a biomass 
loss of 25 -30% of the total lignocellulose in hardwood materials. Moreover, HMC purification and conversion 
processes, were constrained to the single unit operation level, using conventional batch, stirred tank and distillation 
methods, leading to high equipment and space costs, and low process efficiencies Finally, although platform  
molecules from sugars have been identified and studied for quite some time, the process have mostly focused on 
delivering monomeric products, and customer/final market available products. 
 
Contrary to the SOTA approaches , BioSPRINT intends to take a holistic approach by sequencing and 
integrating the best available technologies for each individual step to create a complete value stream from 
waste HMC side streams to biorenewable resins, and deliver PI gains along the enti re HMC biorefining 
process at both individual unit operations and at the entire plant level. 
  
This leads to the conclusion that the realisation of BioSPRINT will have a revolutionising impact on the entire 
HMC biorefining process and, by extension, to a large portion of biorefineries and wood and pulp industries 
processing lignocellulosic biomass , where HMC is one of the fractions extracted,  if not currently valorised . Its 
original and pioneering approach is implemented through the use of beyond the state-of-the art technologies and the 
development of appropriate intelligent tools.  
 
BioSPRINT will provide new and innovative pathways in addre ssing materials, energy, cost and at large, resource  
efficiency questions, and new market ready products in biorefineries valorising HMC by incorporating novel 
methodological approaches and technologies that make BioSPRINT a pioneer in four dimensions: 
▪ Dimension 1: Upstream purification using spinning disc precipitation and hybrid membrane technologies; 
▪ Dimension 2 : Catalytic conversion  using agitated cell reactors for intensified catalytic reactive -extraction 
methodologies for dehydration of sugars into furan monomers; 
▪ Dimension 3 : Downstream purification processes of converted sugar streams, streamlined, improved and 
intensified by acc eleration of liquid -liquid separation and leveraging the integration of thermal units, their 
potential merging, and modularisation for the advanced furan monomer purification methods for the (economical) 
maximisation of final product yields; 
▪ Dimension 4: Polymerisation and production of novel advanced bio-renewable resins using continuous intensified 
reactor technology. 
 
The SOTA and the analysis of the pioneering work within these streams is presented in the following paragraphs. 
 
1.4.1.1 Innovation Stream 1: Upstream Purification using SDR and hybrid membrane purification methods.  
SOTA: Currently, the most commonly used method for upstream purification of C5 and C6 HMC streams involve 
lignin precipitation, dilution and concentration. Existing tools and methods present the following limitations: 
▪ Energy intensive due to the use of high-temperature processes, e.g. distillation; 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  26 
▪ Generation of very diluted streams via addition of large amounts of solvent to precipitate lignin; 
▪ Limited resistance of the membranes towards the prevailing operation conditions (particularly pH and fouling); 
▪ Low water and solvent recovery rates. 
Concerning the lignin precipitation using SDRs and anti-solvent methods, this method of precipitation is less energy 
intensive and more easily scaled -up compared to traditional techniques of cooling or evaporative methods 54 for 
achieving high supersaturations to initiate precipitation. However, dependi ng on the desired size of particles, the 
provision of good levels of either micromixing (or mixing at the molecular level) or macromixing (mixing at the bulk 
scale which affects residence time distribution) is key when contacting the solvent and antisolven t streams55. The 
SDR offers significant flexibility in achieving either mixing conditions in thin films with large contact surface area 
through high shear micromixing and plug flow regime. It is also easily scaled up to process higher volumes of material 
without sacrificing the benefits of thin films and large surface areas. It is envisaged that these processing advantages 
of the SDR applied in conjunction with antisolvent techniques for lignin precipitation can lead to the implementation 
of an efficient, flexible, continuous process whereby the lignin particle size can be tailored to meet the needs of the 
downstream filtration step for high recovery of C5 and C6 sugars.  
The membrane technologies allow selective removal of the impurities and HMC concentration without additives, at 
milder operation conditions, with lower energy consumption and with easy scale up. Hybridisation helps to overcome 
the challenges of the individual separation technologies while intensifying the total separation. Differe nt separation 
mechanisms of the separation technologies and different membrane types allow high separation of different 
compounds, thus increasing selectivity and producing the target HMC stream with high purity. Fractionation of the 
compounds enable their further reuse. 
 
With regards to water and solvent waste, closing the water and solvent loops decreases the environmental load of the 
biorefineries as well as benefits the economy of the industry. Currently, the potential water and solvent recovery rates 
are established at 60% and 80% on average, respectively.  BioSPRINT aims to improve the water efficiency by 
increasing the recovery and reuse rates of the process waters by 90%. The process water also contains valuable 
compounds to be recovered, e.g. residua l sugars, furfural, 5 -HMF and aldaric acids. The hybrid membrane 
technologies enable the efficient recovery and recirculation of process waters and valuable compounds back to the 
processes inside the biorefineries, aiming at zero liquid discharge.  
 
BioSPRINT ambition for upstream purification : T o realise an efficient, integrated membrane/precipitation  
process operating in continuous mode to maximi se recovery of valuable sugars/oligomers , water and resource 
efficiency by increasing the recovery and reuse rate of the process water and of solvents to 80-90%.

1.4.1.2 Innovation Stream 2: Catalytic conversion using agitated cell reactors for intensified catalytic 
reactive-extraction methodologies for dehydration of sugars into furan monomers  
 
54 Fern, J. C. W. et al. (2018) Int. J. Pharm., 543, 139– 150. 
55 Mersmann, A., (199) Chem. Eng. Process. 38, 345–353.

This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  27 
SOTA: The catalytic conversion of sugars into furans (furfural and 5-HMF) bio-based monomers of sugars currently 
relies on dehydration steps in which a homogeneous acid catalyst is used to convert a single type of sugar, e.g. 
fructose or glucose pre-isomerized into fructose58. While such processes are well known, the selectivity is often poor 
and current industrial demonstration units make use of hydrothermal technology, which although more selective, is 
also more expensive, hazardous and energy intensive. Alternative processe s coupling solvent extraction with the 
dehydration reaction for in situ isolation of the reactive intermediates have also been reported in literature, however 
again only for pure glucose/fructose feedstocks at lab -scale using batch processes 59. Existing too ls and methods 
present the following limitations: 
▪ Non-linear relationship between catalyst properties and performance of the conversion process makes prediction 
of optimal catalyst formulations challenging; 
▪ The reactive nature of the target products leads to rapid product degradation due to important gap between reaction 
and separation steps; 
▪ Hydrothermal methods conditions are harsh on common heterogenous catalyst materials and supports, leading to 
fast catalyst degradation; 
▪ Catalysts and solvent extractio n methods for mixtures of sugars and non -glucose/fructose feedstocks have not 
been widely addressed; 
▪ Mixing and mass transfer tend to be poor in batch and conventional continuous processes. 
 
Improvement of catalytic processes such as those proposed in the sugar conversion processes is a complex task and 
involves catalyst design to address both conversion of a multitude of sugars present in the feedstocks, and operating 
conditions optimisation. Catalyst and process design should therefore be carried out simultaneously because changing 
operating conditions or feedstock composition may also change catalyst behaviour. Along the operating conditions, 
catalyst performance depends on many factors and thus finding the best or even a suitable catalyst for processes may 
not be an easy task. However, the relationship between catalyst properties and performance is usually nonlinear and 
not clear. Thus, a lot of time -consuming experimentation is needed if one desires to map this relationship  properly 
optimising the processing conditions in the same time. BioSPRINT overcomes this issue by applying a combination 
of rapid, HTP catalyst screening and ML algorithms to select the catalyst with the best characteristics in a time -
efficient manner for further processing and validation in scaled-up process development.  
Product degradation can occur at high operating temperatures and pressures and when in prolonged contact with  
catalyst/solvents in the reaction medium. Sugar conversion based on reactive extraction technological concepts offers 
the opportunity to rapidly remove the product from the reaction medium, thereby limiting the product  degradation 
for maximum selectivity and recovery of the targeted chemical building blocks such as furans. A detailed kinetic and 
liquid-liquid equilibrium thermodynamic analysis will allow BioSPRINT to precisely identify optimal windows of 
operation for conversion and separation of multiple sugars and products. The use of PI methods, particularly ACRs, 
will allow enhancement of mixing and mass transfer rates, while decoupling this from residence time and conversion, 
while will allow not only intensification of the extraction step to boost product recovery, but also displacement of 
equilibrium, solvent savings, selectivity gains, resulting in higher yields.  
 
During the process design, the operability issues of the continuous process i.e. smooth transition between operating 
points and sufficient disturbance rejection properties, are often neglected or considered only after the process design, 
thereby constraining the process control design. More importantly, PI may lead to situation where process control 
and monitoring encounter new challenges. Therefore, an integrated process and control design approach is preferable 
to identify and solve any operability bottlenecks arising in the development of new processes. ADSC, UOULU and 
UNEW, already have experience of applying such an integrated process design concept for industrially relevant solids 
handling applications as part of the recently completed IbD project. 
 
BioSPRINT ambition for Catalytic conversion: 
1. To surpass the leading existing conversion processes for HMC sugars by developing new catalysts , which 
can robustly function in various media, feedstocks and process conditions;  
2. To intensify key conversion and in situ separation processes and avoid their development in isolation , thus 
lead to an operational cascading process whereby biorefinerie s can convert not only the main input of biomass 
feedstock, but also the primary and subsequent side streams.

1.4.1.3 Innovation Stream 3: Downstream Purification Processes of Converted Sugar Streams.  
 
SOTA: Commercial furfural production processes present comparatively poor yields (3 –10%), high energy-
consumption (wood distillation) and combust the rest of biomass (under-valorisation). The downstream purification 
processes used to convert pentose/hexose sugars into the monomers of furan-based polymer materials is presently 
non-existent, while assorted biomass -based 5 -HMF production operation  currently relies on the numerous 
purification techniques, which aim at isolating 5-HMF alone separately.60 The downstream purification processes of 
HMF include dewatering, extraction and crystallisation, which, e.g., would not be possible using a mixture of sugars 
due to the differences in the precipitation of various furan chemicals (furfural, HMF, homologues, etc.). Analogously, 
pure furfural is being produced worldwide, while it relies on pure feedstock streams, ordinarily thorough selective 
HMC decomposition, utilizing the rest of biomass mostly for energy only. 61 In these cases, however, process side 
streams are not considered for valori sation, while whole biomass resource, often wood, is rather considered as the 
feedstock for furfural, practically excluding any industrial symbiosis with existing paper and pulp industry or bio -
refining. Dewatering and distil lation are the downstream separation process of choice for isolating furfural and 5 -
HMF. Such processes involve the use of large process units, are slow and due to the use of high temperatures and 
long processing time, incur large energy costs and product degradation, leading to low yields. While these mentioned 
methods/technologies are key to achieving specialised furfural/HMF production, these are difficult to combine with 
waste streams. Whereas there are some limited reports on sugar mixture dehydration, 62 examining how to tackle 
downstream for realistic side/waste stream feedstock resources. These streams are not only complex in composition, 
but they tend to vary over time, which lead to process fluctuations from upstream, reactor, downstream, all the way 
to final polymer product quality specification. Therefore, processing bio-refining side streams presents the following 
noted limitations to downstream: 
▪ Existing downstream technologies are developed for comparatively pure streams63; 
▪ As there is no prevalent need for integration, intensification64 is rarely applied; 
▪ There is practically no available data considering real complex streams/mixtures; 
▪ Long processing times and high temperatures lead to product degradation. 
BioSPRINT addresses all these challenges. BioSPRINT will introduce 3 different PI approaches downstream. It will 
generate realistic technical data, translatable to (other) bio-refineries, and make use of plate PI methods to accelerate 
liquid-liquid separation and heat transfer, while minimising the temperature of the contact surfaces. Separating furans 
from low -boiling organic media due to implementing reaction –separation ( PI) is presently at TRL 1 –3, while 
BioSPRINT downstream process with integration and intensification will elevate it to TRL 5. 
 
BioSPRINT ambition for downstream purification: To develop the needed downstream processing for realistic 
dehydration product mixtures. By doing so, to develop  new downstream technologies, capable of minimising 
integrated energy consumption, GHG and costs, associated with downstream separation processes, while intensifying 
separation rates and minimising product degra dation Lastly, to develop, engineer and validate  the first 
downstream, suitable of producing polymer grade furans.

1.4.1.5 Innovation Stream 4: Polymerisation and Production of Novel Advanced Bio-Renewable Resins Using 
Continuous Intensified Reactor Technology    
SOTA: In order to be competitive, the customer price of the final product has to be equal or lower compared to the  
conventional products, or alternatively, the quality of the BioSPRINT products has to be higher than in commercial 
benchmarks. Public acceptance of new products and processes, even the environmentally friendly, is also not self-
evident. BioSPRINT contributes on sourcing the chemical industry with bio-based building blocks. This will support 
the growth of public awareness of more sustainable consumer products with smaller carbon footprint and 
environmental impact. BioSPRINT will prove the sustainability of the proposed value chain in WP 6 and pay special 
attention to wide dissemination of the outcome. This ensures products with high public acceptance. Polycondensation 
reactions such as those proposed in this project are typically mass transfer limited processes where the volat ile 
condensation product such as water or some other small molecule must diffuse out from the increasingly viscous 
polymer mixture into a controlled atmosphere, usually vacuum or nitrogen. The influence of the diffusional process 
on the reaction kinetics is well established from early literature65. The reactor technology commonly used in industry 
is still based on the conventional batch stirred tank technology which has modest mass transfer and mixing 
characteristics. The residence times are high, often in e xcess of 10 hours at larger scale and controlling the product 
quality relies on implementation of tightly controlled operating conditions such as controlled reagent addition and 
reactor temperature and pressure.66  
 
With regards to the polymerisation processes, they present the following limitations: 
▪ Rapid viscosity buildup with increasing monomer conversion, giving greatly reduced mixing, mass transfer and 
heat transfer capabilities as polymerisation progresses.  
▪ Non-uniformity in temperature at higher viscosity causes broadening of the molecular weight distribution and 
consequently poor product quality.  
▪ In extreme cases of total loss in temperature control, runaway reactions can occur causing significant safety issues.  
 
BioSPRINT addresses these limitations: 1) BioSPRINT will evaluate the benefits of continuous thin film processing 
in the SDR in order to maintain high mixing, mass and heat transfer rates and achieve higher and controlled rates of 
polymerisation e ven at high monomer conversions. 2 ) BioSPRINT will aim to generate polymers with uniform 
properties which should be easily tunable by varying the operating conditions in the continuously operated SDR. 3) 
With the reduced processing volume of hazardous materials in the intensified SDR, BioSPRINT will provide a much 
safer process technology.  
 
BioSPRINT ambition for polymerisation: To formulate resole and novolac-type resins and polyols that present 
distinct market advantages over their fossil -based equivalents, also making the production more efficient by 
leveraging PI methods that accelerate polymerisation and reduce energy needs. 
 
The following table (Table 1.10) provides an overview of the SOTA and BioSPRINT progress.

A polymerisation yield > 85% will be targeted as it will guarantee minimum amount of residuals and waste products, 
maximised final polymers performances and REACH compliancy of the resulting products. All the new derivatives 
will be designed and developed trying to maximise their final bio-content, targeting at least a 65% bio-based content. 
Final bio-content will be initially estimated on the basis of each product recipe/composition and then confirmed 
accordingly to ASTM D6866 (quantification of 14C) applied on the most promising candidates selected on the basis 
of their performances when tested in reference formulations. More indicators will be used to validate the key features 
of the new derivatives developed within the project, with particular attention to their thermal stability, fire resistance, 
chemical and hydrolysis stability and processability (e.g. low viscosity for easy handling during processing). In this 
case, thermal stability will be monitored by thermo -gravimetric analysis (TGA) determining the maximum 
temperature at which a certain weight percent loss occurs (e.g. 20%). Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC; ASTM 
D3418) will be adopted as well, to determine glass transition temperatures and polymers crystallinity, if applicable. 
Fire resistance will be assessed as per vertical UL -94, targeting a V0 (burning stops within 10 seconds after two 
applications of ten seconds each of a flame to a test bar and without any flaming drips). Chemical and hydrolysis 
resistance will be measured accordingly to ASTM methods (ASM D543 and ASTM D570). In all the cases, 
commercial benchmarks will be selected as references. Processability will be mainly defined on the basis of physic-
chemical properties of the selected candidates, for instance considering low viscosity values (as per ASTM D2196) 
and reactivity (ASTM D2471 and ATM D7487 for thermoset resins and polyurethane systems, respectively).

on the one hand, and polymer formulation specialists (AEP, PFR) on the other hand, with specific prospects for new 
business, employment and income opportunities.  
 
Market demand & opportunities. Currently, bioplastics represent about 1% of the ~335MT of plastic produced 
annually. But as demand is rising and with more sophisticated biopolymers, applications, and products emerging, the 
market is continuously growing 67. According to the latest market data compiled by European Bioplastics in 
cooperation with the research institute nova -Institute, global bioplastics production capacity is set to increase from 
around 2.11MT in 2018 to approximately 2.62 MT in 2023. Innovative biopolymers are the main drivers of this 
growth in the field of biobased  plastics, which are used in an increasing number of markets, from packaging  (~65 
percent (1.2MT) of the total bioplastics market in 2018 ), catering products, consumer electronics, automotive, etc. 
At the end of the value chain (end -users), the economic value of the worldwide markets that could be accessed by 
the target products (novolac-based resins, bio-based polyols and bio-based foams) of BioSPRINT project is therefore

1.4.3 Patent Search and Freedom to Operate 
For a project like BioSPRINT to be successful, it is important to secure and conserve Freedom to Operate. 
BioSPRINT recognises that patents play a key role to gain a competitive edge and also acknowledges that it is 
Freedom to Operate (FTO) that secures the ability to perform a particular commercial activity of technologies and 
services wit hout infringing third party’s valid Intellectual Property (IP) rights.  The BioSPRINT consortium has 
therefore carried out an FTO search at proposal stage in each technological focus area of the project to become aware 
of third-party IP which are enforceabl e in a specific country in which the foreseen BioSPRINT innovation may be 
commercialised. Among others, the patent search returned patents US7,683,142B2 (March 2010); US 2010/0166870 
A1 (July 2010) ; US 7,615,096 (November 2009) ; US7,273,570 (Sept 2007); WO0218328 (2002); WO0048728, 
WO0048729, WO0048731, WO0048732 (2000) which are either not held in the EU (China, Australia, United States) 
or not considered as blocking due to their scope. Furthermore, there is currently no protected concept on a HMC-
focused biorefining concept. Thus, at proposal stage, no blocking patent was identified . FTO will be monitored 
throughout the project (Task T8.3). In addition, our IP management strategy includes intentions to file patents, which 
will serve to protect the strategic IP brought forward by BioSPRINT (Table 2-8 Patent filing intentions on page 45). 
 
 
Section 2: Impact
2.1 Expected impacts  
BioSPRINT is directly aligned with the fundamental priorities of the BIC SIRA for advancing towards a competitive, 
innovative and sustainable Europe leading the transition towards a bioeconomy, while decoupling economic growth 
from resource depletion and environmental impact . BioSPRINT is a high impact project hinged on intensifying 
biorefining operations for the conversion of hemicelluloses (HMC) so as to i mprove competitiveness, resource & 
energy efficiency; reducing GHG emissions, CAPEX, OPEX as well as for fostering commercial innovation and 
education while being responsive to social factors . As such, BioSPRINT contributes significantly to the expected 
impacts of call  BBI-JU-SO2-R6 ‘Improve biorefinery operations through process intensification and new end 
products’ as described in Table 2-1. Other impacts (OI) are outlined in Table 2-2 on page 37.

▪ contribute to KPI 2 – set the basis for at least one new bio-based value chain; 
Traditionally, the wood industry has always been directed to the production of cellulose in order to produce paper 
or other cellulose derivatives. The rest of the components related to HMC, lignin, and other compounds have usually 
been regarded as having only energy value and used as fuel in most of the industries. As far as HMC is concerned, 
it is still to date a relatively unexploited or under-exploited type of feedstock, from which valuable products can 
however be converted into valuable chemicals. 
BioSPRINT sets the basis of an entirely new bio -based value chain for the recovery and transformation of 
HMC and sugars present in the wood  treatment process of three biomass-derived streams to produce more 
valuable components which will form the raw materials necessary for the development of new bio renewable resins 
and polymers.  The use of compounds initially meant for energy valorisation in the production of high value 
polymers will increase the interest in using natural substances for the production of chemicals usually derived from 
petrochemicals, thus opening the opportunity for the optimal use of renewable sources. 
The new BioSPRINT value chain (described in section 1.4.2, Figure 1.11) spans from raw materials (provided by 
UPM, FhG ad Clariant) to product application, undertaken by AEP and PFR. In this new value chain, not only is 
the biomass feedstock new (in the sense that is wasn’t valorised before) but also the processing (leveraging PI 
techniques and concepts which haven’t been applied in biorefining before and will be advanced using UNEW, NIC, 
UOULU expertise) as well as the end product and application (novolacs, resoles, polyols for wood adhesives and 
PU foam usage). The BioSPRINT new value chain will test and validate its resultant (new) product up to TRL 5, 
thus ensuring it is ready for further demonstration as well as a specified and accepted market application. 
Furthermore, t he BioSPRINT techno -economic and life cycle sustainability assessment will ensure that the 
foundation of the new value chain is economically viable and fulfil all relevant sustainability criteria.  
contribute to KPI 8 – validate at least one new and improved processing technology reflecting the ‘TRL gain’ 
since the start of the project. 
BioSPRINT elevates and validates the level of readiness for 4 groups of PI technologies for upstream purification, 
catalytic conversion, downstream purification and polymerisation to TRL 4/5. As explained in section 1, most 
intensified technologies have been developed to higher TRL levels corresponding to full industrial or demonstration 
scale implementation in non-biorefining applications (chemicals, petrochemicals, food and pharmaceutical), while 
the conventional separation and reaction biorefining methods to be intensified are currently at TLRs 2-3. Therefore, 
by merging PI methods with biorefining applications, the technologies will reach TRL 5 for the majority of the 
process steps, and TRL 4 for the remaining ones, by the end of the project, after simulation, integration  and 
validation for industrial HMC streams in WP5. Details for each group of technology are provided in section 1.3.2.2 
‘Positioning of the project’. Additionally, the process route exploited where HMC sugars are purified and converted 
into furan-based resins is a new technology, alternative to the use of pure sugar feedstocks based on glucose and 
fructose from 1st generation biorefining processes, which compete with food production. 
 
Environmental impacts: 
▪ decrease biomass losses by at least 10% 
BioSPRINT aims to decrease biomass losses by at least 20% from the existing and the most widely used 
pulping processes: Kraft hardwood pulping - currently used by UPM and Organosolv hardwood pulping 
which is investigated by FhG.  For both process types HMC  will be recovered, from the residual streams - 
spent/black liquor streams, at the rate of close to 100%.   
Kraft pulping is the most popular chemical pulping method being used in the pulp and paper mill. This method 
generates high amounts of produced kraft spent liquor, where producing one ton of pulp is accompanied by about 7 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  33 
to 10 tons of spent liquor , which originates from the pulp washing  step. The spent liquor, generated by a typical 
hardwood Kraft pulping, contains about 130 g/l of total dry solids (TDS), volatiles and non-volatiles. About 80% of 
TDS are organic compounds which originate from lignocellulosic biomass, mainly: dissolved lignin, organic acids 
and HMC in a form of hydrolysed monosaccharides (glucose, xylose and arabinose) and their oligomers. Other 20% 
of TDS are inorganic chemicals. T he spent liquor is burnt in the recovery boiler to generate small amounts of 
electricity and steam and to recover the inorganic chemicals for reuse in the pulping unit. The heating value of HMC 
is 21% lower compared to lignin; thus, greater benefit could be derived if HMC is instead used to make higher value 
products such as bio-based polymers or biorenewable resins.

which also represents the decrease of biomass losses in the existing kraft pulp and paper mills.  
In organosolv pulping process HMC recovery from the spent liquor is often not economical  therefore, in most 
cases, it is converted into low value product s such as animal feed or it is digested to produce biogas. HMC makes 
up about 35% of hardwood biomass. During the organic solvent pulping process all the HMC is extracted from the 
biomass. About 60% of HMC ends up in spent liquor - waste stream. BioSPRINT technology can recover close to 
100% of the HMC present in the waste stream and convert it to value products. This valorised HMC represents about 
21% of total lignocellulose biomass. Therefore, BioSPRINT technology will be capable to decrease HMC losses 
from the waste stream of the organosolv pulping process by 100% and to decrease total lignocellulose biomass losses 
by at least 20 %.   
▪ increase the overall resource efficiency; 
Compared to existing biorefining concepts, the BioSPRINT biorefining concept is expected to achieve significant 
increase of resource efficiency by:   
• Increasing biomass efficiency of the existing pulp and paper mills and biorefineries . ~20% of lignocellulo sic 
biomass which is devalued or lost in residual streams will be recovered and upcycled into high value products;  
• Replacing the conventional (batch) stirred tank reactors (STR) with spinning disk reactors (SDR) in the upstream 
purification step and the polymerisation step. A 30% decrease of electricity consumption per weight of processed 
mixture is expected 74.     
• Replacing the conventional (batch) stirred tank reactors with agitated cell (ACR) reactor in the catalytic 
conversion step. A decrease up to 80% of electricity consumption per weight of processed mixture is expected75. 
Replacing STR with SDR and ACR will also result in about 50% reduction of solvents use for precipitation and 
extraction steps which is likely to result in about 50% less steam used for recovery of these solvents per weight 
of processed biomass . Furthermore, an almost 90% reduction of solvents used for cleaning of the reaction 
equipment is expected, which will also reduce the amount of effluent which needs to be treat 
• By applying SD R technology instead of STR for precipitation of lignin in the upstream purification of 
hemicellulose. The consumption of water for this purpose is expected to be reduced by 50%.  
Furthermore, small pulp mills that use non -woody feedstock cannot afford the capital-intensive system in order to 
process the black liquor for energy production and recycling of pulping chemicals. Therefore, many of these small 
non-woody mills are forced to discharge their effluents with no treatments into the DSP. Some of them try to treat 
these effluents with secondary biological treatment and then discharge them downstream 76 . In such BioSPRINT 
technology could bring significant benefits by recovering and converting black liquor chemicals into valuable 
products and increasing the overall resource efficiency.    
▪ reduce the amount of energy required for separation and purification of the targeted products;

Major energy savings, comparing to the benchmark solutions, are expected in all processing step s of 
BioSPRINT biorefinery concept. By replacing the energy intensive evaporation with membrane technology (see 
section 1.3), ~10-30% of energy savings in the upstream purification step (BioSPRINT KPI 1) are expected. These 
assumptions are based on previous experience from FhG and numerous examples in food industry e.g. milk powder 
production or fruit -juice concentrations, w hich show that a single effect evaporation system and a five -effect 
evaporation system consume as much as 600 kWh and 50 kWh per tonne of water removed, respectively. On the 
other hand, revers osmoses (RO) system which is used for water removal, consumes only 10 – 20 kWh per tonne of 
removed water77. Considering the technologies for DSP alone, the reduction of the amount of energy is envisaged 
from ~2000 kJ/kgmixture (the removal of water) to < 1000 kJ/kgmixture (BioSPRINT KPI 7) due to the application 
of reaction–extraction, targeting the polar organic solvents with lower boiling points to shift the equilibrium beyond 
aqueous phase yields. The reduction of energy will also result on account of applying downstream Process 
intensification 1 (see Figure 1.3 on page 14) , namely the thermal unit integration in terms of reactor, as well as 
distillation. For diluted bio -refining streams , only this  grants break -even. The energy saving potential from 
implementing high gravity field technol ogies such as SDRs in the European fine chemicals industry have been 
estimated to be 1-3 PJ of energy per annum and are primarily based on 50% reduction in feedstock, solvents etc. in 
5% of fine chemical processes through more efficient processing. 78 More specifically for polymerisation, the SDR 
has shown promising potential for energy saving of 64% in when converting an industrial scale process for 
polymerisation of styrene from batch to semi-continuous, with a stack of 3SDRs implemented in the high monomer 
conversion stages79. For precipitation processes, the SDR has been demonstrated to significantly improve the product 
properties (almost 2-fold yield increase for TiO2 precipitation) compared to conventional stirred tank reactor (STR) 
at similar power dissi pations80. This implies that same amount of product may be achieved at much lower power 
input in the SDR than in an STR. A similar analysis between T-mixers and SDR highlight the latter to be associated 
with 3 orders of magnitude lower power dissipation wit h little adverse impact on micromixing and nucleation 
kinetics81. The use of plate heat exchangers and heat pipes in distillation column reboiling and condensation units 
can reduce energy consumption in DSP separation by up to 30%82.  
▪ reduce GHG emissions associated with downstream process steps within the biorefinery operations. 
Concerning the technologies for DSP, the reduction of GHG is envisaged from ~0.1 kgCO2/kgmixture (mostly heating) 
to < 0.05 kgCO2/kgmixture (BioSPRINT KPI 7) due to the application of reaction–extraction, targeting the polar organic 
solvents with lower boiling points to shift the equilibrium beyond aqueous phase yields. The reduction of GHG will 
also result on account of applying downstream Process intensification 1, namely thermal unit integration, as well as 
Process intensification 2  (see Figure 1.13 on page 14) , where distillation would be circumvented completely, 
reducing GHG even more substantially. The reduction of GHG emissions from reduced energy consump tion will 
also be complemented from GHG emissions from reduction of solvent use, reduction of waste water, etc.  
Economic impacts: 
▪ decrease overall costs associated with biorefinery processes by at least 10% (…); 
Upstream purification step: In conventional biorefining approach  (see above), HMC and lignin are separated by 
adding about 2 volumes of water to the process stream in order to precipitate lignin. From the remaining liquid 
fraction pre-treatment solvent is recovered by distillation and HMC is concentrated by evaporation. Energy costs of 
the distillation and evaporation units represents the major part of the OPEX of the upstream purification step83. By 
combining SDR and membrane technology to concentrate the liquid fraction and HMC, BioSPRINT will achieve 
significant energy reduction in the upstream purification step comparing to a conventional process. E.g OPEX 
associated with removing 1 tonne of water by 5-effect evaporation system come at cost of about €3.1, while OPEX 
of removing 1 tonne of water by reverse osmosis (RO) system are estimated at about €0.62. Estimated capital cost 
per tonne/hour of water removal for the evaporation system is about €55k, while for RO system is about 30 
k€84.These figures indicate that BioSPRINT technology can potentially reduce OPEX of the upstream 
purification by 80% and CAPEX by about 45% comparing to the existing solutions for HMC purification. 
These potential cost savings are based on the assumption that the same amount of solvent (water) will be removed

as in the conventional step. Nevertheless, the use of SDR for precipitation reaction could pote ntially reduce the 
consumption of the solvent (water) by at least 50% 85, which means that the above-mentioned cost reductions are 
likely to be even higher.      
Catalytic conversion & Downstream processing (DSP) step : BioSPRINT technology for converting HMC to 
chemical intermediates (functionalised bio-based furans) and their recovery in the DSP is also expected to achieve 
significant reductions in OPEX/CAPEX, compared to the conventional biorefining approach. Use of ACR will 
enable catalytic conversion of HMC and extraction of chemical intermediates to occur simultaneously, thus replacing 
the conventional approach of having separate catalytic conversion reactor and the extraction column. ACR has the 
potential to accelerate catalytic conversions due to enhanc ed mass transfer. Faster reaction times will result in 
significantly smaller equipment and make the large -scale production more economically feasible. 86 It has been 
shown in several case studies of catalytic conversion and sugars oxidase that ACR, comparing to conventional STRs, 
can achieve overall energy savings between 88% and 91% estimated on a cost per tonne basis for a typically sized 
production plant. Furthermore, these case studies show that: the consumption of solvents is reduced by 50%, excess 
reactant consumption is reduced by 50% to 70% and estimate that ACR is 3 times more productive per volume 
comparing to STR 87. Use of ACR will also require les s solvent for the extraction which will result in: a) smaller 
equipment for the DSP and b) less energy requirements for recovering the chemical intermediates.  ACR and the 
DSP section are expected to be at least one order of magnitude, in terms equipment footprint and size, smaller than 
the existing conversion and DSP systems. With this downscaling it is expected that the CAPEX of BioSPRINT’s 
catalytic conversion and DSP section will also drop at least 20% to 30%. Furthermore, the energy requirements 
of the DSP, due to smaller volumes of the process streams and milder processing conditions (see section 1.3), are 
expected to reduce from about 2000 kJ/kg mixture (the removal of water) to < 1000 kJ/kg mixture, which is 50% 
reduction of energy. Assuming that energy costs make up about 50% of all operating costs of catalytic conversion 
and DSP step, we conclude that total OPEX of this processing stem will be reduced at least by 25% compared to 
catalytic conversion and DSP solutions of the biorefining benchmark.  
Downstream Purification: replacing the gravity decanter with a hydrocyclone will cut down the residence time in 
the liquid-liquid separation unit for, 30-60 min to <1 min, corresponding to 30-60-fold reduction in equipment size, 
footprint and materials costs. The use of plate heat exchangers in distillation column reboilers can lead up to 30% 
reduction in energy costs, 90% reduction in footprint costs and 50% reduction in equipment costs, 
Polymerisation step: Several studies have reported the benefits of usin g SDR for polymeri sation reactions. 
Significant reductions in processing times, reaction volumes, solvent use and energy requirements are observed in 
comparison to conventional STR technology as well as reduced consumption of the solvent is reduced by as much 
as 50%. In many cases of synthesis of pharmaceutical ingredients SDR offers distinct advantages, in comparison to 
the batch process. At identical production capacity per year: reaction time is reduced by 99.9%, reactor volume by 
99%, and impurity level by 93%.88 In the polymer industry, by replacing typical batch reactors with SDR technology 
it was possible to synthesise the same amount of polyurethane product in 60% less time, using 60% less energy and 
almost hundred times less solvent to clean the reactor91. In case of polymerisation of styrene, replacing batch reactor 
with SDR could reduce energy consumption by 64%. In biodiesel production use of SDR was 8.5 more efficient 
than mechanically stirred reactors 89 . In all examples from the literature SDR achieved higher yields and higher 
purity of the product. Based on these examples the overall OPEX of the polymerisation step is expected to decrease 
by at least 35% - assuming that energy and consumables such as cleaning and reaction solvents costs, which make 
up to about 60% of the OPEX, can be reduced by at least 60%. For CAPEX of the polymerisation step, using the 
same, conservative, assumption as in case of catalytic conversion , it is expected that reactor and product recovery 
can be reduced by at least one order of magnitude resulting in about 20% to 30% of CAPEX reduction. In summary, 
the Estimated cost reductions are as follows:

Fermentation processes are not part of the biorefining process route, Therefore, contribution to this economic impact 
is not relevant for BioSPRINT. Nevertheless, BioSPRINT hemicellulose purification technologies could easily be 
coupled with fermentation processes for C5 and C6 sugars and lead to costs reductions in such biorefining scenarios. 
▪ increase income and business opportunities for stakeholders and actors (includin g primary producers) in 
the bio-based sectors. 
BioSPRINT is based on sound business opportunities along the value chain addressed, and within an economic 
context in which the replacement of fossil-based components with bio-based ones has become an irreversible trend. 
➔ Regarding suppliers of raw biomass,  it must be noted that important income and business opportunities are 
expected to be created for sourcing the biomass in view of its transformation into bio-based polymers and plastic 
materials from renewable sources. The majority of biopolymers are currently manufactured using starch and 
cellulose (from maize, potatoes, and cassava, but also arrowroot, barley, some varieties of liana, millet, oats, rice, 
sago, sorghum, sweet potato, taro, and wheat) 90. By establishing an intensified, sustainable and viable biorefining 
process for HMC into bio-based polymers, BioSPRINT will basically create a new market for the producers of 
lignocellulosic biomass, and more specifically for its HMC fraction, which will be complementary to (not 
substituting) the existing one for the valorised fraction. This is specifically true for the Germany-based hardwood 
and straw producers which supply the lignocellulosic biomass for UPM, FhG CBP and associate partner Clariant.  
Considering that distances and the use of regionally available resources make business more sustainable  and 
environmentally friendly , UPM sources its wood within  a maximal distance of 300km around its planned 
biorefinery91. All job opportunities will therefore be located in rural forest areas. Considering as an example the  
sunliquid® Clariant plant with an annual capacity of 50,000 tons requires approximately 227,000 tons of straw, one 
can measure the extent to which the primary producers of straw could realise new business and income. Based on 
10 percent land use, this gives rise to an area of some 4,700 km² or a collection radius for straw of a good 35 km 92. 
With the HMC fraction now being fully valorisable, BioSPRINT will also contribute to increasing the economic 
value of the raw biomass. 
➔ In the area of industrial biotechnology, significant income and business opportunities are also likely to arise for 
partners providing engineering solutions , consulting  and services as we ll as intensified bio -refining equipment 
manufacturers to biorefining plants, including partners UPM, FhG and EAB member Clariant . Knowhow and 
services will be applicable to multiple biomasses and exploitable through different business models, which include 
licensing of technology, equipment manufacturing and exporting services and knowledge. For example, UNEW, 
NIC and UOULU will be positioned to market PI consulting and engineering services. ADSC and IRIS plan to use 
the know-how and expertise obtained from the analysis, optimisation and scale-up of the main unit reactors such as 
purificators, catalytic convertors, and polymerisation units (WP1-4) to offer services to improve similar equipment 
or industrial processes to companies in the European market. The advantage of using modelling tool such CFD once 
there is a validated model of a process, the analysis of similar processes can be performed in a short period of time. 
Industrial biotechnology currently provides almost half a million jobs in the EU and over € 31 billion in added value. 
By 2030 it may account for between 900 ,000 and 1.5 million jobs and € 57 to 99 billion in added value 93. The 
BioSPRINT partners intend to be active contributors to – and beneficiaries of - the growth of this sector.  
At the end of the value chain (end -users), the economic value of the worldwide markets that could be accessed by 
the target products is extremely large, as indicated in section 1.4.2 (Market demand and opportunities). 
Social impacts: 
▪ improve the operational safety aspects linked to biorefinery operations; 
Inherent safety approaches 94 comprise (a) intensification (less amount of materials, reduced equipment size), (b) 
attenuation (reduction in process conditions), (c) substitution (material, chemistry, processes), (d) simplification 
(remove design complications), (e) limitation of effe cts (design and conditions) and (f) error tolerance (improve 
equipment or handle variations). BioSPRINT introduces 4 key process innovations to address not only a  safer, but 
also more efficient operation. (1) PI technologies designed to operate in continuous mode are associated with smaller 
processing volumes and milder process conditions compared to batch processes, having positive effect on process 
safety (related to all approached a-f). For instance, the application of membrane separations usually means operating 
at ambient temperatures, rather than at the boiling temperatures required for distillation and evaporation processes  
and decreased use of chemicals. In the SDR proposed for antisolvent precipitation and polymerisation, the thin films, 
typically of the order of 50-300 m, represent a very small inventory of material in the reactor at any instant which 
is a beneficial consideration when handling hazardous materials at high temperatures. These films also provide a 
high surface area to volume ratio, typically in the range 5000 -15000 m2/m3, to promote high rates of heat transfer 
from the disc in order to achieve good temperature control in exothermic processes. ACRs will reduce the hazardous 
inventory in reactions and DSP by >50% through combination and extraction technologies, and by enhancement of

mass transfer rates by at least on order of magnitude leading to >50% reduction in solvent consumption. 
Hydrocyclones will reduce the hazardous inventory in liquid -liquid separation by 30 -60-fold and plate heat 
exchangers will reduce the vapour volume in reboilers and condensers up to 90%. (2) Applying PAT, especially 
infrared spectroscopy probes, to measure compositions online (analysis response times ~1 –10 s), thus leading to 
higher process control ( and operational safety due to knowledge gained) . (3) M ulti-scale process simulations, 
provides not only the detailed mechanistic insights into bio-refining units’ design, but also, even more importantly, 
addressing swift (~1–10 min) online mitigations upon encountering the complex unexpected fault in operation. (4) 
In a broader societal sense, operation is safer as an unwanted bio-refining stream is valorised to the products on site 
as opposed to having to be treated by a wastewater treatment plant. Major safety improvements are also achieved by 
introducing new bio-based polymers and biorenewable resins which are less toxic to humans and the environment 
comparing to the existing petroleum -based counterparts. Furfural and 5 -HMF will replace formaldehyde which  is 
highly toxic and volatile and represents direct health and safety hazard to plant operators and product end -users. 
Furthermore, new formulations of resins and polyols will allow a reduction of at least 10% of flame -retardant 
additives, some of which are known to pose a risk of harm to human health and wild animals - persistent organic 
pollutants.95 
▪ create new job opportunities in the bio-based sector, particularly the rural, coastal and/or urban areas. 
Similarly, and in parallel to income and business opportunities, the BioSPRINT project has the potential to be a net 
creator of job opportunities. At the beginning of the value chain (suppliers of raw biomass materials) , particularly 
in the rural areas where the feedstocks are produced , jobs are expected to be created in view of sourcing, growing, 
harvesting, packaging and transporting the biomass from renewable sources. Job opportunities are also foreseen for 
the sustainable management of the forests and woody areas. By offering a new m arket for the producers of 
lignocellulosic biomass, and more specifically for its HMC fraction, BioSPRINT should be the starting point of a 
large job creation phenomenon. After the end of the project, it is estimated that 1 new job per €200k turnover created 
(provision of HMC feedstock) will be created in this area, therefore about 50-100 new jobs could be created. In the 
area of industrial biotechnology , significant job opportunities will also be created so that the  partners providing 
engineering solutions, consulting and services as well as intensified bio -refining equipment manufacturers , can 
sustain the increased demand for their products and services . Those will be highly qualified jobs, either based in 
urban or rural areas. As part of the project, ~5 FTE positions are about to be created and, the plan is to maintain and 
multiply those after project’s end once the exploitation plan of the project is fully implemented . At the end of the 
value chain (end-users), highly-qualified jobs for polymerisation specialists, resin formulation specialists, specialty 
chemists, will be created so as to fully exploit the opportunities of valorising HMC streams into novolac-based 
resins, bio-based polyols and bio-based foams). Given the growth of the sector and the demand for such products, 
for which acceptance is constantly growing, numerous possibilities will arise. As part of the project , BioSPRINT 
will lead to 4 FTE creations in this area, with a high domino effect once th e market, distribution and further use 
possibilities are fully explored and pursued. 
2.1.2 Other impacts (OI) 
Table 2-2. BioSPRINT contribution to other impacts 
OI1: Product engineering impact: creation of new bio-based materials to replace fossil-based materials 
BioSPRINT is a project oriented towards the development (TRL3), lab -scale production (TRL4) and validation 
(TRL 5) of new bio -based materials. BioSPRINT indeed sets the basis for the industrial production of bio-based 
resoles, novolacs and polyols as speciality chemicals , to be used in  wood laminates and panels and in  rigid PU 
foams (e.g. for thermal insulation, structural foams, packaging foams). These bio-based materials have the potential 
to replace fossil-based materials and, via the BioSPRINT ILCSA, will have a proven overall better sustainability. 
Indeed, they are foreseen to present:  ✓ Equal or improved efficiency  ✓ Reduced GHG emissions  ✓Durability 
✓Recyclability. In addition, other improved functionalities during use or reuse will be taken into account during 
the ILCSA carried out under the leadership of IFEU (WP5). The TEA undertaken by MAT will aim at supporting 
the market adaptbility and social acceptance for these new materials by showing they meet a clear market demand 
and fulfil all technical, safety and regulatory requirements, are economically viable and match all relevant 
sustainability criteria. 
OI2: Contribution to the European bio -technology and bio -economy sector’s growth , D ecoupling from 
resource depletion and environmental impact 
Bio-based industries are hugely important to Europe. According to the BIC SIRA, the European bioeconomy taking 
in the food, feed, beverage and primary sectors, has an annual turnover of €2.1 trillion. Bio -based industries 
(relevant chemicals, plastics, pharmaceuticals, paper and paper products, forest -based industries, textile sector, 
biofuels and bioenergy) account for €600billion of this total. Moreover, the European bioeconomy provides jobs 
for 18.3 million Europeans, over half of them in primary biomass production (agriculture, forestry and fisheries). 
 
95https://ec.europa.eu/environment/integration/research/newsalert/pdf/persistent_organic_pollutants_towards_pops_free_future_FB19_en.pdf 
 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  38 
Bio-based industries furthermore employ 3.2 million people96. Industrial biotechnology – an important pillar of the 
bio-based industries – provides almost half a million jobs in the EU and over €31 billion in added value.  
By 2030 it may account for between 900 000 and 1.5 million jobs and €57 to 99 billion in added value97.  
By optimising efficient processing for integrated biorefineries through a multidisciplinary research and innovation 
effort, BioSPRINT intends to make a contribution to the growth of the European bioeconomy, and more specifically 
the bio-technology sector. The expected impacts of the project include: 
▪ To decrease overall costs associated with biorefinery processes (see expected economic on page 34) compared 
with current processing solutions; thus, spurring competitiveness gains which can translate into sustainable growth; 
▪ To create tangible income and business opportunities for stakeholders and actors (including primary producers) 
in the bio-based sectors.   
This impact would be achieved in a sustanable way, that decouples growth from resource depletion and 
environmental impact. At the heart of the transition to a Green Economy 98, defined as one resulting in ‘improved 
human well-being and social equity, while significantly reducing environmental risks and ecological scarcities’, 
lies the decoupling of human well-being from resource consumption. This decoupling is actually recognised as the 
key indicator of positive trends in sustainability and resource efficiency 99. Considering the benefits to be 
achieved in BioSPRINT, i.e. reduction in production costs, reduction in capital costs, reduction of GHG emissions 
and waste, increase in resource and energy efficiency, it is easy to understand how BioSPRINT will translate into 
economic, environmental and social benefits and showcase the enormous impacts in terms of 
competitiveness. 
 
OI3: Contribution to Europe’s raw material security  and red uced dependence on non -renewable 
unsustainable fossil-based resources 
BioSPRINT is a project oriented towards the benefit of Bio-based industries that make use of European biomass 
sources and sustainable European  supply chains. As such, BioSPRINT has the capacity to contribute to lower 
Europe’s dependency on imports and contribute to the EU raw material security. With 90 % of Europe’s chemical 
industry feedstocks for non-energy use coming from fossil resources100, access to alternatives such as biomass is an 
important strategic issue. By creating opportunities for the creation of novel biorenewable resole & novolac resins 
and polyols for high volume productions (wood laminates, PU rigid foams),  BioSPRINT certainly contributes to 
reducing the dependence on fossil-based petroleum-derived materials vastly used at present for these products. All 
biomass used in BioSPRINT present the following characteristics:  (a) non-food competing biomass (b) 100% 
sourced from forests a nd plantations certified and 100% traceable;  (c) not harvested from tropical rainforests or 
plantations that were formerly rainforest. (d) not harvested from indigenous lands. (e) sourcing and forestry sources 
that cause zero deforestation. 
 
OI4: Contribution to the realisation of European climate goals 
By replacing fossil -based products with bio -based products, which have a  smaller carbon footprint, BioSPRINT 
inserts itself in the Europe -wide effort to achieve the objective of  a 20% reduction  in GHG emissions by 2020 
(compared to 1990 levels). BioSPRINT will in particular contribute in sectors (chemicals, plastics) for which bio-
based materials have the potential to make a strong statement towards decarbonisation by offering the replacement 
of fossil-based carbon with renewable carbon as raw materials. As an illustration, bio-based plastics can save 26 kg 
of CO2 equivalent per kilogram of plastic compared  with petrochemical-based plastics 101 and BioSPRINT will 
demonstrate the extent of which PI can help realise this impact for bioren ewable resins. The expected impact of 
BioSPRINT includes to reduce GHG  emissions associated with biorefining process (see expected environmental 
impact on page 34). 
 
OI5: Contribution to improving the innovation capacity and the integration of new knowledge 
The BBI JU Work Plan emphasises the need to ‘optimise efficient processing for integrated biorefineries through 
research, development and innovation’, and also highlights that the need to ‘analyse and communicate the impact 
and the added value of the BBI [ projects] to a wide audience of stakeholders’.  A strong scientific impact is 
expected from BioSPRINT. The technical work of the project requires an interdisciplinary approach that can only 
be provided by a combination of researchers, industr ial actors, end -users and support organisations. The 
BioSPRINT consortium was developed in order to balance the contribution of academic scientific contributions 
(FhG, UNEW, NIC, UOULU ), industry -driven innovation ( UPM, AEP, PFR, Clariant), enabling  technology 
providers (ADSC, IRIS, EBOS ) and support organisations (DEC, MAT , IFEU). BioSPRINT provides a cross-
sectorial and transdisciplinary opportunity for all stakeholders to collaborate and share technical knowledge 
and market expertise.

Our vision is to lead the application of PI in the context of biorefining operations, so as to improve the efficiency of 
the purification and conversion of sugars from the HMC fraction of lignocellulosic biomass and their transformation 
into biorenewable resins, and thus contribute to the development of sustainable and competitive bio-based industries 
in Europe . In order to realise this vision, dissemination and exploitation pathways have already been carefully 
planned. The impact of the project will be maximised by the implementation of a Plan for the Exploitation and 
Dissemination of Results (PEDR) , communication, capacity -building and standardisation activities that will 
bring greater visibility to the project and allow its outputs to be leveraged by identified target audiences (TAs), 
listed, with their interests, in Table 2-4. The initial PEDR has been devised to increase the scientific, innovation and 
business outcome from the project as well as its economic, environmental, and social impacts.

Barrier 1:  High risk investments with long Return on Investment (ROI) timeframes 
One of the main obstacles to accelerating resource efficiency impact in the biorefining industries is the high CAPEX 
necessary for any change in the established process base with long ROI. → BioSPRINT will overcome this barrier 
by performing a strong techno-economic a ssessment and I LCSA which will demonstrate that the BioSPRINT 
biorefining concepts are technically feasible and, most importantly , economically viable. The D&C, TECBP and 
exploitation activities will ensure that a broad community of stakeholders learn s about the project’s results and 
identify exploitation opportunities, including transferability & replicability potential. Furthermore, the technologies 
leveraged in the BioSPRINT biorefining concept can be adopted gradually, thus reducing this barrier. 
Barrier 2: Regulatory complexity and policy enabling framework 
Regulations and standards can represent a massive ally for the implementation of new technologies related with the 
intensification of the Bioeconomy. Regulatory aspects can , however,  also be a barrier to process innovation, 
especially in highly regulated industries like Bio-chemicals. In addition, a supporting and inducing policy framework 
is necessary for fostering reduction of GHG emissions, energy and resource efficiency initiatives, and the creation 
of green growth and jobs. → An overview of the most relevant regulations for BioSPRINT is provided on page 23. 
T6.5 will specifically include a review of regulatory requirements to ensure future compliance. We have also 
included, as part of our D&C efforts (WP7), engagement and outreach efforts towards policy makers considering 
that possible public incentives will be key in enabling the adoption of the BioSPRINT research & innovation.  
Barrier 3: Lack of consumer awareness and acceptance for bi o-based products and applications:   
Understanding and responding to consumers’ needs, concerns and perceptions about  bio-based products and 
applications is key to achieve better awareness and acceptance for bio-based products. ➔ BioSPRINT reckons that 
establishing a high level of transparency  is important. This is the reason why the ILCSA will aim at  recording 
certified (positive) impacts of the BioSPRINT bio-based products and processes, together with information on the 
full value chains from feedstock  cultivation to application, and their benefits in terms of sustainability, etc., and 
make these results publicly availab le, preferably in databases, where possible. We have also included, as part of 
our communication efforts (WP7), to reach out to the media and general public  and the possibility to cluster with 
CSAs looking into these topics (e.g. BIOBRIDGES).

Dissemination strategy. The dissemination strategy is pivotal to the project’s success and will be established and 
managed by WP7 leader Ms. Sarah FUCHS (DEC) acting as the D&C Manager. A detailed dissemination strategy 
and plan will be developed and maintained in task 7.1. DEC has extensive experience in managing the dissemination 
of results from multi -disciplinary, collaborative innovation projects and is well positioned to outreach and  engage 
with the European Bioeconomy actors. DEC will be supported through the co -operative work of the BioSPRINT 
partners who will participate and have been allocated effort to promote the project results within their respective 
communities with great att ention paid to complementarity and multi -dimensionality of actions.  Dissemination 
activities will be reviewed, evaluated and refined throughout the project to ensure the effectiveness of each individual 
action. There is a strong mutuality between dissemination of project results and communications activities (described 
in Section 2.2.4). We will employ both traditional channels such as conferences (presentations, papers and posters), 
publications in peer -reviewed and open access journals, dissemination in industry magazines, newsletters and on -
line platforms, as well as mor e vanguard channels, such as social media platforms ( Facebook, Twitter, LinkedIn, 
ResearchGate, Academia.edu, YouTube), etc.  
 
Dissemination practises. The consortium commits to the following practices: 
▪ All presentations, contributions and publications, even partially funded by the project, shall include the project 
logo and prominently acknowledge the EU H2020 grant under which the project is funded. 
▪ Details of all publications, even partially funded by the project, shall be uploaded to spe cific and agreed 
bibliographic networks such as Mendeley, ResearchGate, GoogleScholar. Every publication will carry an agreed tag 
specific to the project, e.g ‘BioSPRINT’. An explanation of how to access such publication lists by tag will be added 
as a reminder in every report. Additionally, whenever bibliographic networks allow for a catalogue of publications 
to be retrieved by tag or published as an RSS feed, the project's website will expose such a catalogue. 
▪ All project-related presentation materials shall be published on the project's web site under a Creative Commons 
license or another appropriate license and made available through social data-sharing channels (e.g. SlideShare). 
▪ Datasets shall be published, when appropriate, on the EU Open data portal and linked from the project's website. 
▪ All publications shall be published in line with the project’s Data Management Plan (DMP) and the project’s IP 
Management Strategy, described below, thus ensuring protection for the project’s IP, personal data, etc.

How the proposed measures will help to achieve the expected impact of the project.  
Through dissemination and communication activities, BioSPRINT’s underpinning purposes are to : 1. Increase 
awareness about the tangible and intangible outputs (knowledge, technologies, cooperation, etc.) of the BioSPRINT 
RIA; 2. Make these outputs available to TAs best placed to build upon them and advance their technological, 
scientific, business or policy work; and 3. Broaden their impact beyond the original project focus. In this way we 
aim to avoid duplication of efforts, and c ontribute to knowledge building and advancement of innovation in 
Europe’s bioeconomy. Particular attention will be paid to ensure the relevance and application of the BioSPRINT 
biorefining concept to bioeconomy actors, starting from biorefineries. In addition, all dissemination activities will 
be developed with an eye towards the exploitation of the project results by other parties, to lay down the basis for 
subsequent innovation actions as well as other private initiatives which can accelerate the uptake o f PI solutions in 
general and the BioSPRINT outputs in particular.  The fact that the BioSPRINT PEDR includes multidimensional 
actions aimed at training & education (the BioSPRINT TECBP, spanning three years of the project with materials 
remaining available and exploitable after the end of the project), standardisation (the CWA is aimed at evolving 
towards a Technical Committee on its own after the end of the project), regulatory compliance, wide dissemination 
through publications, workshops, conferences, is a testimony of our commitment to maximise the impact of the 
project while giving due consideration to its follow-up. 
The partners’ own contribution to D&C activities, in which all partners are involved, and for which all partner s 
have been allocated effort, will help achieve the expected impact of the project. One key aspect of this relates to the 
strong industry engagement in BioSPRINT which is truly aimed at contributing to the exploitation and deployment 
of results. The following table indicates the industry engagement for the maximisation of the BioSPRINT impact:

2.2.3 Business Plan – Beyond the Project’s Lifetime 
The BioSPRINT biorefining concept is inspired by a ‘ holistic process intensification (PI) philosophy’ which we 
strongly believe will support a paradigm shift in the refining of HMC streams in the European bioeconomy . The 
relevance of the project’s innovation to a wide range of target audiences (see section 2.2, Table 2. 4) underpins the 
commercial exploitation of BioSPRINT, beyond the project’s lifetime. In addition to developing a unique intensified 
biorefining concept for HMC streams, the project also aims to pave the way for realising a direct economic benefit 
through the identification of early adopters and post-project opportunities as part of T7.3 in WP7. This is the reason 
why the task will also include, by M48, the development of a business plan for the exploitable results of BioSPRINT. 
The following sections aim at presenting our approach and initial thoughts for the implementation of a strategic 
business plan that can drive long-term commercial viability beyond the project’s lifetime. All partners will contribute 
to deliver high impact exploitation pathways in line with their activities and business strategies (Table 2.7, page 42). 
The core exploitation activities will focus on the B ioSPRINT intensified biorefining concept, for which a business 
plan ( D7.10) will be developed , as part of T7.3 . This task will study  customer segments (e.g. enterprise users, 
consultants, equipment manufacturers, research & academic institutions) , describe a  product and services with 
investor and end-user credibility, and expand on aspects such as sales strategy, marketing strategy, explicit channels 
to market, pricing strategies. It will be validated with data from industry participants in the project (e.g. willingness 
to pay & revenue model validation, customer segments validation / market validation, and channels validation) which 
have commercialisation experience (e.g. UPM, PFP, IRIS), under the leadership of MAT . The partners who will 
primarily own the BioSPRINT foreground IP, i.e. mainly UNEW, NIC, FhG, UOULU and UPM, are committed to 
Finland ,  the Slovenian Research Agency, etc.). Target values: > 2 opportunities for further funding 
identified and explored, as part of the project, by the Innovation & Commercialisation Board. 
H-Standards 
bodies  
 
BioSPRINT aims at participating to the establishment of standards related to process intensification and 
the bioeconomy. To this end, the project’s outputs related to standardisation will be disseminated to 
CEN/CENELEC, ISO, etc. A CEN Workshop Agreement (CWA) on ‘PI for Biorefining Processes’ will 
be established. Mr. Mavroyiannos, Standards officer, Cyprus Organisation for Standardisation, member 
of the EAB, will support these efforts. The CWA will remain active even after the end of the project.
