AEP Exploit the BioSPRINT results though further collaborative research; licensing of optimised recipes for the production 
of novel bio-based resins and polyols. Prioritise exploitation activities towards exploitation of a complete technology 
platform for the conversion of HMC into bio-based resins and polyols. 
ADSC Generate further knowledge and R&D efforts on biomass systems that would help increase expertise in this 
competitive sector. Integrate the knowledge gained into its portfolio of engineering services for analysis, optimisation 
and scale-up of biorefining equipment. 
EBOS Extend the capabilities and functionalities of EBOS ML algorithms for predictive maintenance and apply those to 
other applications. Advance its knowledge in industrial technologies, thus extending the company’s R&D activities 
through further collaborative and own research. 
FhG Exploit the project’s results via further internal and/or collaborative research, expanding its expertise mainly in the 
field of downstream processing and isolation/purification of valuable compounds from sugar and wood and producing 
publications and/or patents related to the investigations made by FhG CBP. 
IFEU Exploit the BioSPRINT results in view of improving and expanding its ILCSA methodology. Leverage the knowledge 
and expertise gained in new collaborative projects which require an ILCSA and/or provide it as a service to industrial 
R&D endeavours.  
IRIS Undertake further internal research on monitoring technologies applied to bio-based processes, products and streams, 
thus increasing IRIS know-how (knowledge and IP) in this area. 
NIC Proceed with further internal research/collaborative research: exploring the synergies with other BioSPRINT project 
partners, commonly addressing (development) challenges. Efforts will take the form of new projects commercial 
industrial services in multi-scale process modelling or PI; publications and patent applications. 
MAT Expand its portfolio of modelling, simulation, process design and techno -economic feasibility studies and services, 
targeting public and private companies which run biorefineries or plan to invest in biorefineries and PI technologies 
such those developed in BioSPRINT.    
UOLU Exploit the knowledge and IP via further internal and collaborative research in which separation techniq ues and 
advanced data analysis methods are developed and applied to develop novel bioproducts . Publications as well as 
development work on separation processes (R&D) with companies (biorefineries, paper mills, chemical industry etc.) 
for global markets. Transfer the research results into its education materials for BSc and MSc programmes.  
PFR Exploit existing know - how in development of tailor - made resins for creation of bioresins according to real 
requirements of the market for PF resins. Check suitability of actual utilisation in the industry for construction, 
insulation or industrial application   
UNEW Establish a long -term collaboration with the industrial partners in and beyond the project. Contribute to the 
development of a technology transfer and IP exploitation plan in collaboration with other project partners and exploit 
the knowledge and IP gained via consultancy contracting and R&D projects.  
UPM Further evaluate the efficiency and economic gains that would be made possible by transposing the BioSPRINT 
biorefining concept into its biorefining plant under construction. Investigate the incorporation of the BioSPRINT 
results into industrial activities either via collaborative or self-funded demo and prototyping activities at large-scale. 
 
Key exploitable results. The primary exploitable result of BioSPRINT is the technologies, in 4 focus areas, which 
intensify the valorisation of HMC streams into added-value compounds and products. The following sections provide 
an initial overview of the opportunity.  Competitive Landscape & Market Opportunity. Currently, there are 224 
biorefineries in Europe102 out of which 25 are wood-based biorefineries (excluding pulp for paper only) producing 
 pulp, tall oil, specialty cellulose or bioethanol and 5 produce cellulosic fibre or bioethanol from other lignocellulosic 
feedstock such as wheat straw, Miscanthus or switch grass . This creates a strong European market for biorefining 
technologies in Europe. At global scale, t he global market for Biorefinery technologies will grow from ~€423.5 
billion in 2016 to ~€648.6 billion by 2021, with a compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of 8.9% for the period of 
2016-2021103. Similarly, with Europe, it can be assumed that l ignocellulosic biorefineries currently represent ~ 10-
14% of all biorefineries globally. Thus, the market can be estimated as representing ~€66.9 billion by 2021. This is 
sizeable yet realistic market for BioSPRINT. As for new products, recent analysis projects the market share of bio-
based chemicals in the global chemical industry to increase from 2% in 2008 to 22% by 2025, and the market potential 
for bio-based chemicals will reach ~€17.8 billion by 2016104. The need to substantially deliver smaller, cleaner, safer 
and more energy -efficient technology that allows to ‘close the loop’ a nd also v alorise sugars from HMC, into 
innovation bio-based chemicals, is one important priority for the European bioeconomy. BioSPRINT is in a unique

2.2.2 Knowledge management and IP Protection Strategy 
Intellectual Property Rights (IPR) Management during the project. For the success of the project it is essential 
that all project partners agree on explicit rules concerning IP ownership, access rights to any Background and Results 
for the execution of the project and the protection of intellectual property rights (IPRs) and confidential information 
before the project starts. Therefore, such issues will be addressed in detail within the Consortium Agreement (CA). 
The main purpose of the CA is to establish a legal framework for the project in order to provide clear regulations for 
issues within the consortium related to the work, IP  ownership, Access Rights to Background and Results for the 
duration of the project and any other matters of the consortium’s interest.  
Access Rights to Background and Results. In order to ensure a smooth execution of the project, the project partners 
agree to grant each other royalty-free Access Rights to their Background and Results for the execution of the project. 
The CA will define further details concerning the Access Rights to Background and Results, after the project’s end.  
IP ownership. In line with the default for H2020, results shall be owned by the partner carrying out the work leading 
to such results. If any result is created jointly by at least two partners and it is not possible to distinguish between the 
contribution of each of the partners, such work will be jointly owned by the contributing partners. Any joint results, 
including inventions and all related patent applications and IP will be jointly owned by the co ntributing parties. In 
order to further the competitiveness of the EU market, and to enhance exploitation of the consortium results, each 
contributing party to jointly created IP shall have full freedom of action to independently exploit the jointly create d 
results as it wishes (e.g. right to transfer results to their affiliated companies without prior notification to the other 
project partners), while always protecting and assuring the Access Rights of the other project partners. Such use of 
results will encourage competitiveness of the EU market by creating broader uses of the results and opening up the 
markets for the Consortium’s results in all markets, thus supporting the goals of the consortium towards seeing 
commercial outputs evidenced from the BioSPRINT project.  
Knowledge Management and IPR activities  will be addressed by T 8.4 ‘Innovation, IPR & Data Management  ‘, 
led by MAT (Mr. Mladen Crnomarkovic as Innovation & Transferability Manager). The task will develop and finalise 
a detailed Knowledge Management and IPR Strategy, which will be followed throughout the project and closely 
interconnected with the exploitation and dissemination activities paving the way for post -project exploitation. The 
overall BioSPRINT IPR strategy will be to promote the sustainability of project results, considering that any 
interested party can commercialise them while protecting the rights of the consortium partners. An integrated 
approach will be followed for t he identification, capture, retrieval, distribution, sharing, use and reuse of generated 
information and knowledge assets. This will be essential to drive the value and benefits of these assets, and contribute 
to maximising the innovation impact of the action. Knowledge management sessions will take place regularly during 
project meetings. Partners will receive IP training from the Innovation & Transferability Manager  and will sign 
declarations of compliance with the CA IP policies and procedures. Complianc e will be driven by WP leaders, the 
Innovation & Transferability Manager and the project’s Technical Manager. At the earliest possible stage, every 
Figure 2.7. BioSPRINT Business Model Canvas

Open access strategy & Data Management Plan. We will take part in the pilot for open access to research data in 
H2020 and develop open educational resources ( Training, Education and Capacity-building program under task 
T7.2). The above acknowledge the importance to Europe that future projects build on previously funded efforts as 
well as open dissemination. We are also committed to Open access to scientific publications, and as such will commit 
an important proportion of project publications to open access, considering the need to balance openness and 
protection of scientific information, commercialisation and IPR, privacy concerns, security as well as data 
management and preservation questions. The consortium will also use an Institutional Open Repository to store and 
provide access to scientific publications and researchers profiles and automatically feed OpenAIRE with articles to 
ensure the largest possible impact among researchers, policy-makers and businesses. For all project outcomes with 
public level of dissemination, the DMP will determine how wider distribution can be considered, following the H2020 
guidelines (Deliverable D8.1). With respect to data expl oitation, the DMP will determine how other stakeholders 
may exploit the data and how the data can be verified after it has been deposited. We will use established standards 
and non-proprietary formats for data collection as far as possible, to support future exploitation of the data either by 
partners or by external organisations where open access is implemented.  
IP Protection through patents. BioSPRINT partners NIC, UNEW and AEP also intend to file patents on IP which 
they will identify in cooperation with MAT. Strategic IP filings will be directly managed by these IP owners in a way 
that will not impede the underlying aspirations to open science. Consequently, the consortium partners have agreed 
not to pub lish confidential or restricted results under Open Access, regarding all scientific publications produced 
along the project lifecycle, until IP filings have been confirmed in the identified strategic and commercially sensitive 
areas. Specifically, special attention and consideration will be given to IP, information and data characterised as EU 
CONFIDENTIAL, guided by the Innovation & Transferability Manager, in close consultation with the PC.

2.2.4 Communication Activities  
Communication Strategy. Under the leadership of Ms. Sarah FUCHS (DEC), we will  deliver a comprehensive 
communications programme aimed at informing and promote the project and its results and successes by providing 
targeted information to stakeholder groups and audiences, as indicated in Table 2-4 on page 39. This will include 
communication to the media and the general public, in a strategic and effective manner, also engaging them in a bi-
directional exchange. The communication plan and associated activities (e.g. development of the project’s identity, 
brand, website and social channe ls) will be developed in detail under WP 7, as part of the PEDR . The plan will 
incorporate specific objectives and key messages addressed to relevant target communication audience  groups, 
setting out clear descriptions and timings for each activity. The aim will be to ensure the visibility of BioSPRINT 
and its results, ensure they can be understood even by non-specialists, and thus multiplied. The project website 
(developed by EBOS) will be operational by M1 as well as the project’s four social media channel s (Facebook, 
LinkedIn, Twitter and YouTube).  Communication objectives. Based on the target audiences (TAs)  identified in 
Figure 2-6 and their expected interest in the project, the objectives of communication strategy and their relation to 
the TAs are to (a) Provide a clear view of the project, its goals and results (b) Create awareness of the project among 
the full range of stakeholders impacted by the results and engage those in a co-creation approach (c) Create interest 
from an active community of potential end-users (d) Prepare the ground for the dissemination of project's results (e) 
Establish liaisons with other projects, initi atives and bodies for knowledge and innovation transfer  (f) Support the 
commercial exploitation of results. (g) Recognition of the results among audiences beyond the immediate project’s 
reach (standards bodies, policy-making institutions, ETPs, etc.) h) Demonstrate how EU funding tackles societal and 
economic challenges. Communication activities and channels. The integrated approach to communication adopted 
by BioSPRINT combines a mix of traditional and disruptive communications channels as listed in Table 2 -9. All 
activities will be organised in communications campaigns. Communications activities targeting large numbers of 
stakeholders will use “push” communication chan nels with broad reach (e.g. social media, project’s website, 
newsletters) while activities targeting smaller group of stakeholders (e.g. policy-makers, funding organisations) will 
use targeted “pull/interactive” channels such as workshops (invitation-only), restricted meetings, targeted electronic 
communications, etc. A set of modern communication and knowledge management tools will be used along with 
four social media platforms (LinkedIn, Twitter, Facebook, YouTube) as well as document-centric communities and 
forums that have pan-European visibility (e.g. MS Sharepoint, IBM Connections).

Objectives:  
• To perform the analysis of feedstocks (sampled for variation), establishing technical composition specifications, 
and analytical comparisons by exchange of measuring methods between the partners (ring trial); 
• To obtain a stream free of solids suitable to be fed to membranes systems; 
• To reduce unit size, consumption of chemicals and waste amounts by intensification of the precipitation processes 
during purification of hemicellulose (HMC) using SDRs and anti-solvent methodologies; 
• To establish a hybrid membrane purification system for the removal of inorganic and organic impurities and 
concentration of HMC, recovering of at least 80–90% of the HMC weight; 
• To develop robust systems for handling of viscous and solid streams in the process; 
• To enhance water and solvent reuse strategies inside the processes guaranteeing 80-90% recovery; 
• To generate experimental data for the validation of the process system engineering tools.  
Task 1.1 – Analysis of treated hemicellulose (HMC) streams (NIC, DEC, FhG, UOULU, UPM). HMC-rich 
streams, currently produced as non -valorised side/waste streams, will be supplied to the diverse stages in this 
project. UPM will supply a hardwood HMC stream from pilot biorefinery operations. FhG will supply an 
Organosolv stream from a pulping of beech wood pilot plant. A third stream, generated from steam explosion of 
residual wheat straw, will also be supplied by Clariant, from the Sunliquid® demonstration plant. All streams are 
available at monthly to quarterly time intervals in quantities from 10-500L per batch to allow scalability test. The 
characteristics of these streams determine the need, feasibility and selection of upstream separation processes. 
Therefore, the physicochemical properties of the HMC feedstock streams will be determined. HMC sugars and 
degree of oligomerisation, decomposed oligo-/monomer sugars, furans, biomass-derived organic acids, lignin and 
inorganics (ashes) will be quantified using standardis ed wet, spectroscopic and chromatographic methods. 
Transient spectrophotometric data (particularly IR) will also be acquired, processed and correlated with the other 
offline methods for the development of in-line PAT. Additional physicochemical properties required for equipment 
design purposes, such as density, viscosity and surface tension, will also be determined. Data will be collected to 
assess the variability of the streams over plant operation cycles, or the changes of raw material composition, so 
that variability can be accounted for in purification and reactor engineering design tasks, simulation and piloting 
in WPs 2–5. The potential for the valorisation of HMC will be determined by analysis of the whole resource -to-
market chain, based on variable f eedstock compositions, the possibilities for recovery and feasibility. Technical 
feedstock specifications will be generated for the supply of raw HMC streams, processing and quality of the 
purified input batches for the use in applications, developed in WP 4. Inputs will also be provided for the ILCSA 
in WP6. NIC will undertake the analysis of streams which will serve as foundation for further technical work in 
the project, assess robustness. DEC will liaise with UPM, FhG and Clariant. UOULU will specifically leverage its 
existing expertise in PAT, providing time-on-stream information to the operators of bio-refineries.  
 
Task 1.2 – Precipitation and solids handling (UNEW, UPM, FhG). HMC streams can contain large amounts of 
lignin, which is typically removed through dilution -precipitation, or in the case of FhG, through distillation -
concentration. Such methods generate large amounts of waste water, are energy intensive, and offer poor control 
of particle size and morphology, cr eating difficulties for solids handling. Intensification of lignin and/or HMC 
removal from the feedstock will be achieved using SDRs (for control of particle size) and anti-solvent precipitation 
methods (to minimise the amounts of waste generated ). The pre cipitation and solids handling processes will be 
designed by UNEW in close collaboration with FhG and UPM. 
ST1.2.1 Benchmark tests using dilution and distillation precipitation processes [FhG]: Current HMC purification 
methods developed at FhG will be used. Precipitation of the pre-treatment extract through ethanol distillation for 
water concentration will be used as a benchmark for comparison with intensified processes in WP1. 
ST1.2.2 Intensification of precipitation using SDRs [UNEW]: Green antisolven ts and salting agents will be 
selected. The selection will be studied, benchmarked and optimised for product purity. Intrinsic kinetic information 
will be measured for use in simulation studies in WP5. The anti-solvent precipitation methods will be intensified 
using SDRs to create a compact and zero -heat process. Reactor configuration will be developed for continuous 
processing. Operation conditions will be screened to enable scale up. The nature of the precipitates will be analysed 
using spectroscopic and chromatographic methods and correlated with the process design and operating conditions. 
ST1.2.3 Optimisation of particle formation for enhanced solids recovery [UNEW]: Optimisation of experimental 
conditions will be done using DoE methods. Solvent and sal ting agent amounts will be minimi sed, product

BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  49 
recoveries maximised. Particle size and morphology will be tuned to relieve solids handling challenges in the SDR 
precipitation process to allow the stream to pass through the SDR for as long and uninterrupted durations as 
possible, easy filtration for solids removal, and also minimise fouling in downstream of the SDR in the membrane 
purification step (T1.3). Particular attention will be paid to maximise recovery and sugar segregation.  
 
Task 1.3 – Hybrid membrane purification and concentration of HMC sugars (UOULU, FhG, UNEW, UPM). 
T1.3 will focus on membrane purification as an intrinsically intensified separation technology. The objective is an 
efficient recovery and purification of the HMC sugars. To prevent membrane fouling and to ensure the efficiency 
of membrane separation process, removal of undissolved wood fibres and any pre-precipitated lignin by filtration 
will also be tested. The targeted feed streams for membrane purification will contain sugars, dissolved HMCs, 
organic acids, and small amounts of soluble lignin, hydrocarbons,  volatiles and inorganic compounds. The 
purification needs and the methods for individual streams will be evaluated. The FhG HMC stream is relatively 
pure, requiring only the removal of acetic acid and residual ethanol, and concentration through water removal. The 
HMC stream from UPM stems from solid -liquid separation after the pre -treatment step without any additional 
purification. Hence, it contains large amounts of soluble lignin, furans, organic acids, soluble ash, and pre -
treatment chemicals. T1.3 will be led by UOULU in collaboration with FhG, UPM and UNEW. Membranes will 
be tested alongside conventional precipitation processes to create hybrid system and intensify purification. 
Feedback will be received from WP2 to enhance the removal of catalyst inhibitors. Sustainability aspects will be 
considered when selecting separation units and feed into WP6. Attention will be paid to minim isation of energy 
and reduction of the number of units. 
ST1.3.1 Identification of suitable purification process es [UOULU]: Different membrane materials available at 
UOULU and FhG will be tested and evaluated, and the best will be selected. Micro-, ultra- and nano filtration or 
reverse osmosis membranes will be screened, and optimal performers further tested for lifetime and fouling. The 
effect of pH adjustment will be investigated since most of the membranes do not tolerate relatively low pH . The 
pH of most HMC streams can be as low as 2 or 3. In addition to C5/C6 sugar purification, such developments will 
also allow us to challenge the conventional lignin removal methods and test membrane purification for the 
precursor streams without lignin precipitation, concentration and dilution precipitation. 
ST1.3.2 Hybridisation of the upstream purification [UOULU]: Hybrid processes where membrane purification is 
combined with conventional purification processes may be needed. We will explore how the streams may be 
fractioned by combining micro-, ultra- and nanofiltration in series (UOULU) and different membrane steps with 
distillation and extraction methods (FhG),  thus enabling the recovery and valorisation of the other value -added 
products.                                         
ST1.3.3 Optimisation of the process conditions [UOULU]:  The process operating conditions (e.g. temperature, 
pressure, superficial flow velocity, disturbing compounds ) will be evaluated and optimi sed, utilizing a MODDE 
DoE software to quickly and comprehensively find out the combined effects of different variables. 
 
Task 1.4 – Identification of upstream scale-up challenges via process systems engineering (PSE) tools (IRIS, 
ADSC, UOULU, UPM). The upstream purification processes resulting from the lab experiments (T1.1-T1.3) will 
be analysed using PSE tools. The CFD model will be utili sed to design the process hydrodynamics to prevent 
concentration polarisation and fouling of the membranes and to enhance mixing in SDRs. Control analysis and 
PAT techniques will be used to identify scale -up recommendations. Together, these PSE tools can prevent 
shortcomings of the scale-up of the intensified units from laboratory to pilot scale. A set of recommendations for 
the successful scale-up process will be formulated. T1.4 is led by IRIS, with CFD/PAT and industry expertise from 
ADSC, UOULU and UPM.  
Task 1.5 - Solvent and water recovery (UOULU, FhG) 
ST1.5.1 Solvent recovery [FhG]: Following optimisation of solvent amounts in S1.2.3, solvent recovery and reuse 
will be investigated using membrane and distillation processes. Solvent degradation and accumulation of inerts 
and undesirable species over multiple operation cycles will be investigated. 
ST1.5.2 Water recovery [UOULU]: To improve the water efficiency , the recovery and reuse of water streams 
originated from different stages of the purification process will be investigated by UOULU in collaboration with 
FhG. Simultaneously, the v aluable compounds (e.g. residual sugars, furfural, 5 -HMF) will be recovered and 
circulated back to the process.  Hybrid separation technologies such as membrane technologies, adsorption and 
advanced oxidation processes (e.g. photocatalysis, catalytic wet -air oxidation) will be evaluated to ensure zero 
liquid discharge and oxidise small quantities of harmful organic molecules and precipitate harmful cations (e.g. 
heavy metals). A photocatalytic membrane unit developed at UOULU will be utilised.  
 
Role of the main partners: UOULU leads WP1, overseeing the PI of upstream purification, mainly supported by 
UNEW, NIC and IRIS. Justification of resources: UOULU and UNEW will assign 2 persons over a 30-month 
period 50% time). NIC and IRIS will assign 1 person @ ~5PM/person/year.

Objectives:  
• To develop robust predictive models for catalyst composition and performance with at least 95% prediction 
accuracy through combination of ML and HTP catalyst development methods; 
• To develop scalable catalysts for simultaneous C5/C6 sugars dehydration (selectivity >90% and full conversion) 
• To develop kinetic models to describe the conversion and aqueous-organic partition of multiple C5/C6 sugars; 
• To intensify the dehydration reaction by integration of the conversion of multiple C5 and C6 sugars to furans in 
a single unit, while limiting side products to a maximum of 40%; 
• To reduce losses of furan reactive intermediates by 20% through intensification of the dehydration reaction step 
by development of a continuous extractive-reactor unit; 
• To investigate the effect of upstream purification efficiency in catalyst lifetime and production yields; 
• To develop strategies for integration of recycled solvent streams from WP3.

Task 2.1 - Catalyst Development using HTP Methods (UOULU, EBOS, NIC, UNEW). Due to the complex 
nature of HMC sugar systems where multiple C5/C6 sugars are present, individual sugar isolation and conversion 
is not feasible. BioSPRINT will deliver robust catalyst formulations for integrated conversion of C5 and C6 sugars 
into a single reactor, coupled with extraction of reactive furan intermediated. Attention will be drawn to catalyst 
scalability, poison resistance, hydrothermal resistance, lifetime and use o f affordable and abundant chemical 
elements. HTP and ML methods will be used to develop predictive models that can accelerate catalyst development, 
identify non-obvious optimal formulations and easily allow catalyst tuning for different feedstock compositions. 
ST2.1.1 Creating of catalyst libraries for testing [UNEW]: Libraries of solid acid heterogeneous catalysts will be 
created by UNEW though HTP synthesis methods. This will be complemented by testing of commercial ion 
exchange resins for comparison. The process will follow an iterative approach to build on foundation formulations, 
and add catalyst functionality through use of promoters, in parallel with testing in ST2.2.2 and predictive modelling 
development in ST2.2.3. The materials created will be char acterised (e.g. using spectroscopy, X-Ray Diffraction, 
Ion-Coupled Plasma Spectroscopy, and physi/chemi-sorption methods) and a library of measured catalyst properties 
and literature/calculated molecular descriptors will be generated to feed into the ML algorithms in ST2.1.3.       
ST2.1.2 HTP Testing [UNEW]: Catalysts will be screened using HTP methods to measure catalyst performance in 
conversion of the C5 /C6 sugars identified in the feedstocks used in this project. Feedstock composition , reaction 
conditions and the effect of extraction solvents in the reaction mixture will be screened. Reaction products will be 
measured using GC and HPLC methods. Data sets and performance results will be generated as inputs for ST 2.1.3.   
ST2.1.3 Development of ML predictive Models [UOULU, EBOS]: ML algorithms including variable selection, 
evolutionary optimisation, intelligent model identification and model validation will be developed by UOULU and 
EBOS based on assets from FUNBARK and CHARIOT  projects. The resulted models will be employed to create 
predictive ability between experimental data and accelerate catalyst development and optimisation. This will lead to 
fast identification of catalysts with maximum activity for conversion of complex sugar mixtures with optimal 
This proposal version was submitted by Jochen Michels on 04/09/2019 16:25:03 Brussels Local Time. Issued by the Funding & Tenders Portal Submission System.
BioSPRINT                                        Part B - Sections 1-2-3                            Page  51 
selectivity towards the key products of interest, with the objective being to obtain a catalyst with >90% selectivity 
towards 5-HMF and furfural/leading to max 40% reduction in by-products generation.                                                                                                 
Task 2.2 – Reactions for realistic stream mixtures (NIC, UNEW). While there has been extensive work done on 
the dehydration of sugars, this is rarely considered for realistic stream mixtures, which are complex. Based on 
compositions (from WP1), a DoE will be prepared, including conventional and novel homogeneous approaches (e.g. 
with H 2SO4), introducing solid acid catalysts, evaluating the process in terms of kinetics, using simple but also 
complex mixtures, also considering the effect of intermediates, impurities (e.g. lignin-based) and inhibitors. 
ST2.2.1 Chemical kinetics determination, modelling & simulations [NIC]: These will be evaluated for synthetic-, as 
well as for realistic feedstock mixtures, analysed in WP1 , in order to establish reaction structure –activity 
relationships, which will in tur n be plugged into reactor unit simulation, optimisation, as well as the WP5 -based 
overall integration. An as comprehensive mechanistic scheme as possible will be applied, to not only describe a mix 
of reactants, but also the potential inhibiting effect of impurities (as notable constituents of feedstock). 
ST2.2.2 Integration using synthetic sugar mixtures [NIC]: Synthetic sugar mixtures will be applied before 
proceeding to realistic feedstock compositions in order to individually establish the interactions between the former, 
at first excluding the influences of impurities. Considered sugar mixtures will introduce model pentose-, as well as 
hexose sugars, their type, quantity and ratio being determined based on the upper/lower concentration levels 
determined at WP1. The DoE will consist of 10–20 runs, these also representing a database for ST2.2.1. 
ST2.2.3 Effect of intermediates, impurities & inhibitors [NIC]: Analogously to the main reactant components, 
namely sugars, the key determined (WP1) impurities wil l be added as a spike to the (selected) ST2.2.2 mixtures, 
individually assessing their respective role (inhibiting or promoting). Consequently, realistic feedstock streams will 
be subjected to modelling, collecting the data of the previous subtasks, finalising mechanistic model descriptions, 
ideally independent on the provenience of resource. Stream composition will serve as input in addition to operating 
process conditions (e.g. temperature). 
ST2.2.4 Optimisation for real complex streams [UNEW]: Previously collected data, input into model, will be used 
to optimise the reactions for real complex streams. Optimisation will be executed based on sole reactor objectives 
(conversion, yield, productivity, etc.) (1), considering immediate upstream or downstream ( e.g. the purification of 
by-products) (2), while, as a plug in for WP5, simplifications will be superimposed for integration (e.g. considering 
just principle component impurities) (3). 
Task 2.3 – Development of extractive reaction intensification (UNEW, DEC, NIC). PI via extractive-reaction 
will allow the in situ isolation of reactive intermediates (i.e. 5-HMF and furfural). Selective solvent extraction of the 
furans from the aqueous reaction medium will prevent degradation and increase yields. Reaction-separation coupling 
will allow reduction in capital cost and in hazardous solvents inventory. NIC will focus on solvent selection for 
maximum recovery of target products, while UNEW will ensure optimal integration with the dehydration reaction 
and downstream purification in WP3. Mixing intensification to increase the rates of liquid-liquid mass transfer and 
enhancement of contact with the solid catalysts will be addressed through the use of ACRs which will provide a size 
reduction in process scale and continuous flow. NIC will responsible for the application/extension of the modelling 
from the purely reactive mechanisms to intensified reaction –extraction operation, where biphasic thermodynamic 
equilibria will be more relevant. Existing modelling framework will be  upgraded by component distribution 
coefficients, which will determine the desired mass fluxes of e.g. HMF from the aqueous - to extractive organic 
medium to shift equilibrium. UNEW,  will design the process as a whole.  DEC will ensure  optimal knowledge 
transfer towards WP7 and TECBP. Solvents will be screened, basing their consequent selection on EHS/LCA 
(following e.g. Green Chem., 2007, 9, 927) (1), water/medium 5-HMF partition coefficients (2), as well the potential 
direct implementation of polymerisation (WP4), addressing the proposed PI (WP3), omitting distillation (3).  
ST2.3.1 Extraction Solvent Screening /Evaluation/Selection [NIC]: Solvents will be screened, basi ng their 
consequent selection on EHS/LCA (following e.g. Green Chem., 2007, 9, 927) (1), water/medium 5-HMF partition 
coefficients (2), as well the potential direct implementation of polymerisation (WP4 activities), addressing the 
proposed Process intensification 2 (WP3), omitting distillation (3).                                                         
ST2.3.2 Reaction and Extraction Coupling [UNEW]: UNEW will integrate all operations into a single stage of 
extractive reaction.  ACRs (or Coflore) will be used to enhance mixing, promote plug flow performance and control 
the system selectivity. Reactor residence time distribution and mass transfer chara cterisation techniques will be 
employed to determine the reactor operational performance. Flow information, operational data and kinetic models 
will be experimentally coupled to understand the effects of operatory conditions in the reaction selectivity and yield. 
ST2.3.3 Optimisation of Process Conditions [UNEW]: DoE methodologies will be used in conjunction with CFD 
modelling (T2.4) to optimise ACR geometries & operating conditions, leading to minimal pressure drops and energy 
inputs while maximising mixing & mass transfer efficiencies, leading to higher recovery of target reaction products.  
ST2.3.4 Integration of recycling streams [UNEW]: The product stream leaving the extractive-reactor will consist of 
an aqueous and an organic phase, which will be purified as described in WP3. The re-use of solvent streams recycled 
from the downstream purification will be investigated to identify impact of solvent quality on extractive -reaction 
performance over several cycles. Accumulation of inert or undesired species will be addressed and feedback to the 
development of downstream purification units in T3.2 and scalability studies in T3.4.

Objectives: 
• To separate main, secondary and trace reaction components to a commercial chemical specification; 
• To transfer the already-obtained separation parameters to downstream simulation program ( e.g. CHEMCAD), 
validating the models for reprocessing; 
• To proceed with laboratory, intermediate and pilot PI experiments to recover at least 90% of the weight of 5-
HMF, furfurals by thermal, physical and physical –chemical separation techniques (distillation or rectification 
primarily considered; liquid/liquid extraction or membrane filtration operation assessed in silico ) from the 
organic/aqueous reaction solutions of WP2; 
• To reduce liquid-liquid decanter size by one order of magnitude through intensification using hydrocyclones; 
• To reduce distillation, reboiler/condenser unit size by 80-90% and energy consumption by 35% through the use 
of compact heat exchangers; 
• To generate high TRL (4 –5) experimental data to validate purification process scaling (unit energy/mass 
balances, following lifecycle studies and BioSPRINT exploitation)  
 
Task 3.1 – Preparatory processing actions for downstream  (FhG, AEP, NIC, UNEW) . From WP2, various 
material streams contain ing unreacted sugars, but predominantly 5-HMF/furfurals, will be provided . T3.1 will 
analyse, qualify and quantify the selected valorised products in solutions. Primarily, isolated 5 -HMF and furfural 
will be targeted, while the mixtures of different furan homologues will also be considered. 
ST3.1.1 Integration of downstream with WP2 activities [UNEW]: Differently-composed streams will be transferred 
from WP2 to WP3 to assess separations/purifications in parallel. ST3.1.1 will execute a process sensitivity analysis 
considering composition. UNEW will provide the samples and respective documentation. 
ST3.1.2 Reaction composition analysis for downstream [NIC]: While principal reaction components will be 
measured continuously within WP2, exact composition analyses will be more detailed for the last operation sample, 
corresponding to the composition of the product. Gas chromatography analysis will be implemented, while mass 
spectroscopy will be for more detailed assessments (e.g. impurities and suspected of inhibiting polymerisation). 
ST3.1.3 Process design simulation of distillation [FhG]: Distillation will be considered as the primary separation of 
choice for the WP2 reaction products in the extraction medium. FhG will develop distillation simulation models as 
a preparation for T3.2. Composition data generated in ST.3.1.2 will be used as inputs, specifically, applied organic 
media, as well as the content of 5-HMF/furfurals or/and the presence of impurities. 
  
Task 2.4 – Identification of reaction scale-up challenges via process systems engineering tools (ADSC, IRIS, 
UOULU). The reaction and extraction processes will be analysed using CFD tools at lab-scale. Using lab experiment 
results from T2.1-T2.3, the objective is to  provide a set of recommendations on how to scale -up an intensified 
reaction and extraction reactor. These will include the thermo -fluid behaviour of the reactor using CFD tools, the 
control analysis and PAT techniques on the later-on pilot plant equipment. The task will be undertaken by ADSC in 
close collaboration with UOULU, IRIS and with PI expertise provided by NIC and UNEW. 
 
Role of the main partners:  UNEW leads WP2, overseeing the intensification of catalytic conversion, mainly 
supported by UOULU, NIC and ADSC. Justification of resources: Over a 30-month period, UNEW and NIC will 
assign 1 person (full time) while UOULU and ADSC will provide 1 person (50% time).

Task 3.2 – Development of distillation (with/w/o extraction) (NIC, FhG). Reaction data from T3.1 will be input 
into a process simulator (CHEMCAD or Aspen Plus) to proceed with design and costing of downstream units. WP2 
product streams will be separated on a lab scale unit (TRL 3–4) by FhG. Batch and continuous operation modes to 
separate fura n products and recover solvent will be tested. FhG has the know -how for the separation of 
furfural/carboxylic acids from mixed biomass streams. For this, the (vacuum) rectification/ distillation in L/kg scale 
are also available. Also, liquid/liquid -extraction can be performed to selectively separate the furan derivatives 
especially HMF. An optimised process concept will be developed to minimise energy, waste and emissions. 
ST3.2.1 Bench scale tests of downstream [FhG]: FhG will implement the 1 st set of downstream separation 
experiments, separating primarily 5-HMF/furfural from the extractive-reaction solvent, applied in WP2 (including 
the selection of media ). UNEW will continuously  report on the use of these to facilitate the development of 
distillation to follow thereafter. Based on solvent/furfurals boiling points, the implementation of distillation will be 
considered as either batch or continuous in terms of rectification – depending on final product purity, as well as 
energetics. 
ST3.2.2 Modelling validation, simulation and optimisation [NIC]: The modelling of distillation will be executed in 
order to expand the range of conditions, compositions and purities to be predicted by simulations. Conditions, media 
and products will be rated in terms of energy/mass balance equations, thermodynamics (e.g. the equations of state) 
and available equipment characteristics (e.g. dimensions). In addition to single step distillation, a combination with 
extraction will be considered solely in silico in order to evaluate potential processing benefits in terms of purity, 
energetics or costs – specific local optima will be validated (ST3.2.1). 
ST3.2.3 Scale-up to pilot unit operation [FhG]: Initial modelling (NIC) will provide a support to the scale-up to TRL 
4–5 operation (FhG), as factors, such as fluidics, monitoring and control will be considered. Pilot scale validation 
will be impl emented by FhG, based on multi -scale modelling simulations, started by NIC  and finalised by FhG. 
ST3.2.3 will feed into T3.3, as well as, the unit scale-up considerations beyond BioSPRINT (T3.4). Resulting pilot 
data will be evaluated via plant simulation software, already used in previous WPs. Results will be transferred to 
WP5 for energy/mass balance, technology lifecycle and operation cost studies. An aim will be to prepare the reaction 
conversion solutions with the described downstream processes through  piloting; products will be able to be used 
directly/in the sufficient batch quantities by the users for applications (WP4). 
 
Task 3.3 – Intensification of downstream purification processes  (UNEW, DEC, FhG, NIC). Intensification of 
downstream purification will be focused in two key areas: a) the reduction in foot print and hazardous inventory for 
the liquid-liquid decanter unit that separates the aqueous and the organic phases originating from the extractive -
reaction unit and b) intensification and integration of heat transfer in distillation units. 
ST3.3.1 Intensification of Liquid-Liquid Decanting [UNEW]: Decanting of liquid-liquid mixtures is a slow process 
dependent on the acceleration of gravity, requiring long residence times and large decanter volumes. Hydrocyclones 
will be used to exploit the increased acceleration fields due to centrifugal forces and to cut down on residence time 
by at least an order of magnitude. 3D printing methods, guided by CFD designs, will be used for fast prototyping of 
geometrical shapes to optimise liquid-liquid separation. 
ST3.3.2 Heat transfer intensification with integration [ FhG]: Reboilers and condensers in distillation columns are 
energy intensive. Additionally, some of the reaction products, e.g. 5-HMF, decompose easily at high-temperatures. 
Therefore, it is important to minimise reboiler residence times and temperature driving forces. This will be achieved 
via PI using plat heat exchangers and heat pipes (UNEW). Strategies for near -isothermalisation will be developed 
using these devices. Plate geometries will be developed to enhance phase -change rates. Thermal unit integration, 
applying the data of pilot separation campaigns will be performed by FhG. Integration with the reaction upstream 
will be considered, primarily in terms of recuperating the energy for the latter from rectification.  
 
Task 3.4 – Identification of downstream scale-up challenges (UOULU, IRIS, ADSC). Downstream purification 
processes will be analysed using the CFD tools at lab scale operation  (T3.2). Along with CFD, the results from 
control/PAT techniques, performed herein will be used to identify scale-up recommendations. Together, these PSE 
tools can prevent some typical shortcomings of the scale-up of the units from the lab to larger scale. Finally, a set of 
the recommendations for the successful scale-up process of downstream purification processes/separation PSE will 
be formulated. T3.4 activities will be undertaken by UOULU in close collaboration with ADSC and IRIS. 
Role of the main partne rs: NIC leads WP3, overseeing the intensification of downstream purification, mainly 
supported by FhG and UNEW. Justification of resources: NIC will assign ~1 person (full time) over a 26-month 
period while FhG and UNEW will assign 1 person (@~5-7PM/person/year).

D3.1 Methodology overview report on downstream purification [NIC, M1 5] A report, covering the downstream 
purification of furan-based monomer mixtures, where various separation strategies are considered. 
D3.2 Initial downstream unit distillation operation design models [NIC, M 18] A document, containing the 
description of the initial downstream unit operations by modelling and experimentation. 
D3.3 Final downstream unit distillation operation design models [NIC, M3 1] A d ocument, containing the 
description of the downstream unit operations by modelling and experimentation. 
D3.4 Report on intensification of separations [UNEW, M32] A technical report document with a demonstrator on 
the intensification of liquid–liquid separation, heat transfer, and efficient thermal integration.

Objectives:  
• To convert the monomers obtained in WP3 (furfural, 5-HMF) into novel novolac-and resole-type resins (500 g 
scale) for most promising applications e.g. in wood laminates and panels; 
• To convert 5-HMF into novel Mannich-base polyols (500 g scale) for rigid polyurethane foams; 
• To characteri se key properties of the various types of resulting bio -based polymers benchmarking versus 
commercially available counterparts in reference formulations; 
• To intensify the processes for the production of resins and polyols; 
• To optimise and replicate the best reaction syntheses (1-3 Kg/batch) to enable further process upscaling 
 
During the early stages of the project, PFR and AEP will use commercially available counterparts of the mentioned 
monomers to synthesise the new polymers and polyols, shifting to the monomers purified in WP3 as soon as they 
will be available in progressively increasing amounts, starting from M15. The comparison based on QC data of 
different monomers’ batches will allow synthetic protocols optimisation as well as the definition of raw materials’ 
average specifications. The polymers and polyols thus obtained will be tested in reference formulations, addressing 
wood laminate panels and polyurethane rigid foams, according to reference recipes and benchmarks of industrial 
use. A first upscaling (1-3 Kg/batch) of the best syntheses will be carried out, fine tuning the recipes, when necessary, 
to determine the process parameters and provide data to WP5. 
Task 4.1 - Development and testing of novolac-and resole-type resins and their applications in wood laminates 
(PFR, AEP, NIC). Furfural and 5-HMF, once characterised for their purity, will be used as potential replacements 
for formaldehyde in the synthesis of resole and novolac-type derivatives. Furfural and 5 -HMF charge will b e 
adjusted to evaluate their contribution to final polymers’ physico-chemical properties (e.g. color, viscosity, average 
molecular weight distribution) and reaction kinetic (e.g. conversion monitored by chromatography methods). 
Reagents’ purity contributio n to process conditions and resulting polymers average QC properties will be 
investigated using model syntheses to verify if a drop-in approach is possible or if tailored adjustments are required. 
At the same time, furfural and 5-HMF will be compared to the same molecule already commercially available, used 
in the same recipes; the average QC properties of the resulting derivatives as well as any significant variation to 
reaction conditions will be then compared to determine if any significant difference ha s occurred. The newly 
developed resins will be characteri sed for their QC properties (residual water content, hydroxyl value, viscosity, 
pH), reproducibility and regulatory status (REACH compliancy) and tested in reference industrial formulations for 
thermosetting wood adhesives. Biorenewable resins will be compared to existing products for different applications. 
For most suitable application dedicated resin will be developed and checked  in respect to processability and 
properties of resulting material. 
 
Task 4.2 - Development and testing of bio-based polyols and their applications in rigid PU foams (AEP, PFR, 
NIC). A similar approach will be adopted for the development of polyols for PU applications, where furfural and 5-
HMF will be used in the synthesis of novolac -type and Mannich -type polyols. Novolac -type polyols will be 
synthesised following similar conditions as above, but adding an alkoxylation step (preferably in liquid phase with 
the use of ethylene, propylene or glycerine carbonates) to introduce different types of reactive hydroxyl groups 
(primary or secondary). Self-catalytic Mannich polyols will be also prepared by reacting 5 -HMF with phenol-type 
monomers (petro or bio -derived) and suitable  alkanolamines (e.g. diethanolamine, ethanolamine, 
diisopropanolamine), through a one -step (direct alkanolamines addition on phenols) or a two -step ( through an 
oxazolidine intermediate) route. An alkoxylation step (still in liquid phase) will eventually be added, if necessary, 
to tune final polyols’ reactivity. Following the same principle as described in T4.1, the same recipes will be used, 
but replacing furfural and 5-HMF with formaldehyde (or paraformaldehyde). In this way, a direct comparison of 
possible advantages or limitations deriving from the replacement of reference materials (e.g. formaldehyde) will be 
accomplished. All products will be characterised for their QC properties (residual water content, acid value, hydroxyl 
value, viscosity), reproducibility and regulatory status (REACH compliancy).  
 
Using reference formulations from industrial applications (e.g. thermal insulation, structural and packaging foams), 
the new polyols will be benchmarked versus commercially available fossil-derived polyols characterized by average 
similar QC properties, in order to quantify their advantages and easily connect them to market demand. The best

formulations will be used to prepare lab scale demonstrators representative of real rigid PU foams, which will be 
characterized for their performance properties, like reactivity, density (ASTM D1622), dimensional stability (ASTM 
D2126), compression strength (ASTM D1621), wat er absorption (ASTM C272), thermal conductivity (ASTM 
C177), fire reaction properties (UL-94 horizontal and vertical burning tests).  
 
Task 4.3 - Process intensification of bio-based polymer production (UNEW, DEC, AEP, PFR, NIC). Based on 
the formulations at T4.1 and T4.2, which will be studied in conventional batch STR technologies, protocols will be 
developed to transfer the optimised formulations to a continuously operated polymerisation process with a view to 
upscaling the production of selected polymers from grams to kg scale. We will evaluate the performance of a thin 
film SDR for both types of polymerisations. The enhanced mixing, heat and mass transfer capabilities enabled by 
the intense fluid dynamics of the thin film flow should encourage more intimate contact between the reacting species 
especially in highly viscous polymer environments. Polyols are particularly promising candidate reactions for PI in 
the SDR. As an equilibrium -controlled process which is driven by continu ous removal of water or other small 
volatile molecules from the polymerisation, the polyol formation step can benefit from processing in the SDR. The 
continuous, easily controllable thin film formed on the rapidly spinning disk should enable more efficient  water 
removal and faster kinetics, especially if the viscosity of the mixture increases at high monomer conversions. We 
will thus exploit the SDR thin film characteristics with its well-established intense gas-liquid mass transfer rates to 
speed up and optimise the rate of the polymerisation reaction by continuous, selective evaporation of water to drive 
the reaction to completion . This will be investigated at a range of disc temperatures identified in the formulation 
tests and with a counter current flow of inert gas such as nitrogen to aid removal of water. UNEW labs are equipped 
with a range of SDRs of disc diameters between 0.1 to 0.3 m representing lab scale to pilot scale units which will be 
used for this study. Various disc surface geometries will be  tested (smooth, grooved, and mesh layers) under 
conditions of a single or multiple disc passes. It is envisaged that optimised conditions (geometry, operating 
temperature, feed liquid flowrate, inert gas flowrate) will be thus be identified which gives th e maximum 
improvement in the performance of the polymerisation reactions in terms of higher yield, lower operating 
temperatures and faster reaction times. We will apply similar considerations to promote the efficient mixing of liquid 
reagents employed in t he novolac-type polymer resin formation in order to enhance the polymerisation kinetics. 
Attention will be paid to the potential fouling of the surfaces in contact with the polymer; fouling mitigation 
strategies involving for example the incorporation of a non-stick surface compatible with the processed reagents will 
be considered during the design of the surface geometry to be implemented in the SDR. 
Task 4.4 - Identification of polymerisation scale -up challenges via PSE tools (IRIS, ADSC, UOULU). The 
polymerisation processes will be analysed using CFD tools at lab -scale. Recommendations on how to scale -up an 
intensified polymerisation reactor will be formulated and include the thermo-fluid behaviour of the reactor using 
CFD tools, the control analysis and PAT techniques on the later-on pilot plant equipment. T4.5 will be undertaken 
by IRIS in close collaboration with ADSC, UOULU and with industrial expertise provided by AEP and UNEW. 
 
Role of the main partners: AEP leads WP4, overseeing the intensification of the polymerisation stage and the 
production of bio renewable resins, supported primarily by UNEW, NIC & IRIS. Justification of resources: Over 
21 months, AEP will assign 1 FTE while UNEW, NIC & IRIS will assign 1 person (@7-9PM/person/year).

Task 5.1 – Simulation and analysis of the existing and improved biorefinery processes (MAT, EBOS, FhG, 
UOULU, UNEW, UPM). Led by MAT, T5.1  focuses on assessing and improving the overall performance of the 
BioSPRINT PI biorefinery concept. Process simulation models of existing and new biorefineries will be developed 
for FhG Organosolv, UPM, and Clariant’s Sunliquid. The simulation models of the existing biorefinery (ST5.1.2) 
will be the benchmark for KPIs of the intensified biorefinery concepts. The KPIs will be established in ST5.1.1 and 
will be used to monitor the improvements achieved in the PI concepts, specifically the reduction of OPEX, CAPEX, 
energy and water consumption, and the yield improvements. Simulation models of the improved biorefinery 
concepts will be developed in ST5.1.3, and used for performance analysis and technoeconomic optimisations during 
the ST5.1.4. Task 5.1. will closely interact with WPs 1-4, which will provide the inputs to develop and validate the 
simulation models. The results will in turn valuably support the process development in WP1 -4. This interactive 
approach will focus R&D where improvement matters, saving experiments and pilot tests. T5.1 will interact closely 
with T6.3, where the biorefinery cost model will be developed. UOULU, UNEW  and EBOS will support the 
development and testing of the models, and assist in linking the results from the plant-wide biorefinery simulations 
and work conducted in WP1 -4 and WP6 -7. The Clariant model will be built by collecting publicly available 
information on Sunliquid process105,106. FhG and UPM will provide the information necessary to model their 
biorefinery process as close as possible to the facilities under study. 
ST5.1.1 Defining biorefinery process boundaries & collection of input data [MAT]: Standard biorefinery process 
will be described in terms of technology, products, wastes stream, process conditions and energy consumption.  
Process boundaries will be setup to include key technological steps relevant for BioSPRINT. Physical properties 
data will be collected for nonconventional compounds. MAT will source data from literature and its own database 
as well as relevant information provided by industrial partners UPM and FhG, whose teams will guide in preparing 
the initial process flow diagram and mass and energy balances. KPIs will be established and defined.     
ST5.1.2 Building simulation model of the existing biorefinery (baseli ne) [MAT]: Using results from ST5.1.1. a 
simulation model of the baseline biorefinery will be developed and validated using industry standard software 
AspenPlus. The KPIs for the baseline biorefinery will be calculated by the simulation model (D5.1).      
ST5.1.3 Building simulation model of improved biorefinery (PI technologies implemented) [MAT]: Simulation 
models of the intensified biorefinery concepts will be developed. These will be robust models capable of detailed 
mass and energy balances accounting for variable feedstocks, plant capacity, wide range of process conditions and 
different scenarios combining multiple PI technologies. The KPIs of the new biorefinery concepts will be calculated, 
and performance analysed to optimi se the design in ST 5.1.4. The main outputs will be: Overall mass and energy 
balance (process, waste and utility streams); Insight in technology; Identify limitations, risks and opportunities of 
different technology implementation and upscaling; Support for scale-up activities, process parameter optimisation 
and control strategy; Preliminary equipment design of process and non-process (outside battery limit) for full scale 
capital cost estimate; Capital investment costs, fixed and variable costs per process; Identification of cost dr ivers; 
Input for risk analysis (technological, technical, economic); and Base for energy analysis/optimisation. 
ST5.1.4 Performance analysis and optimisation of improved biorefinery [MAT]: The simulation models developed 
in ST5.1.3 will be used to analyse the performance of the new biorefinery concepts under different operating 
conditions and for different capacities. Various configurations of technologies across the entire process will be 
assessed for technological and economic viability. The analysis will  focus on identifying risks, limitations and 
optimisation opportunities of the PI technology. These studies will provide quantitative targets which new PI 
technology needs to meet to be economically viable and to provide the required improvements in energy and water 
footprints, operational safety and costs. The results will support the evaluation of business opportunities in T6.3.           
 
Task 5.2 – Integration of the developed concepts and technologies for UPM stream (UPM, AEP, IRIS, MAT, 
NIC, UOULU, UNEW). The individual technologies developed in WP1 -4 will be integrated to process >100L of 
UPM feedstock. The best concept of intensified technologies (determined in T5.1) will be sequentially tested using 
the pilot equipment available at each par tner. The KPIs and process simulation models established in T5.1 will be 
validated at pilot scale. The scale-up challenges identified at T1.4, T2.4, T3.4 and T4.5 will be addressed and data 
to deploy further scale-up activities in future exploitation proje cts will be collected. UOULU, UNEW, NIC, AEP, 
IRIS and MAT  will support the integration of upstream, reaction and downstream processes according to their 
expertise in WPs 1-4. Due to nature of UPM pre-treatment and current process, emphasis will be put into validation 
of the upstream purification technologies for removal of lignin and potential catalyst inhibitors. Upon completion, a 
technology-transfer report will be produced. 
Task 5.3 – Integration of the developed concepts and technologies for Organosolv stream (FhG) (FhG, AEP, 
ADSC, MAT, NIC, UOULU, UNEW). This task will follow a similar aim, ambition and structure to T5.2, but using

FhG feedstock. Due to the nature of the FhG Organosolv process, emphasis will be put into validation of reaction 
and downstream purification technologies. Upon completion, a technology-transfer report will be produced. 
T5.2 and T5.3 follow the same subtask structure, indicated below: 
ST.5.2.1, ST5.3.1: Pilot and validation of Intensified Sugar Purification [FhG]: Feedstocks produced at UPM/FhG 
will be pilot tested for lignin precipitation at FhG/UNEW, and membrane purification at UOULU/FhG using the 
best technologies and operating procedures developed in WP1 and T5.1, adjusted for stream composition.  
ST5.2.2, ST5.3.2 Pilot and validation of intensified monomers production and purification [UNEW]: The purified 
sugar streams generated in ST5.2.1/ST5.3.1 will be pilot tested by UNEW and NIC using the ACR technology and 
best operating procedures developed in WP2 and T5.1 will be adjusted for stream composition. For these sub-tasks, 
the pilot kit will be stationed at FhG to allow seamless integration with the downstream purification processes. The 
reaction product streams will be processed at FhG on the best distillation technology available as specified in WP3 
and T5.1, out of the various pilot plants available, which have 1-100 separating stages, and can process 1–63 L/h. A 
100 L liquid/liquid extraction plant with counter current flow is also available. 
                     
Task 5.4 – Transferability case study with Clariant Sunliquid® stream (DEC, MAT, NIC, UOULU). A case 
study report on processing the Clariant stream with the recommendations on the integration of the most efficient 
process operation variants will be produced. It will be elaborated choosing the best PI technology options, catalytic 
reaction processes, and downstream unit operations, including and analysis of industrial symbiosis possibilities. 
ST5.4.1 Feedstock variability study with comparison [DEC] : All key characteristics for consequent 
upstream/reaction/downstream processing ( HMC, decomposed oligo -/monomer sugars, furans, biomass -derived 
organic acids, lignin, inorganics (ashes), density, viscosity, mixture surface tension, etc.) will be compared to those 
of the previously analysed streams in order to pinpoint the most vital differences to consider.  DEC will collect the 
data from WP 1–4 activities, in order to prepare a summary characteristic comparison. 
ST5.4.2 Transferability tests for intensified technologies [UOULU]: An assessment of the possibility of replication, 
transfer or adaptation will be prepared. Process sensitivity analyses will be performed in silico  by varying the 
different operating conditions or compositions for an integrated upstream/reactor/downstream unit operation series, 
inputting Clariant specifics. If needed, selected intensif ied runs will be performed to more accurately assess 
transferability. All attained outcomes will be communicated to Clariant continuously to get feedback on constraints. 
Task 5.5 – Scale-up of optimal product formulations (AEP, IRIS, UNEW, PFR) The most promising polyols, 
pre-polymers and resins produced by AEP and PFR will be  selected, characteristics benchmarked,  and their 
developed syntheses replicated through the multiple repeatable batches in small–medium bench scale (1–3 kg) in 
order to analyse optimal process conditions, assess average standard QC properties, and define preliminary tentative 
specification, thus setting the premises for the scale-up to a near-industrial pilot level. Data will be shared with 
T4.4/4.5 to support the modelling, simulation and scale-up of polymerisation reactor units, for pilot plant engineering 
and optimisation enduing. 
ST5.5.1 Pilot resins validation using UPM feedstock [AEP]:  Optimal WP4 resin formulations, based on UPM 
feedstock will be considered, focusing on batch scaling/ repeatability robustness, as well as encountered deviations.  
ST5.5.2 Pilot resins validation using FhG feedstock [AEP]:  Optimal WP4 resin formulations, based on FhG 
feedstock will be considered, focusing on batch scaling/ repeatability robustness, as well as encountered deviations. 
ST5.5.3 Optimisation of polymerisation intens ification technology [UNEW] : Intensified polymerisation 
implementation will be paralleled to non-intensified ones comparing key performance indicators. 
Role of the main partners: UPM leads WP5, overseeing the Validation, Process Simulation & Integration of the 
BioSPRINT processes, mainly supported by MAT and UNEW. Justification of resources: Over 37 months, UPM 
will assign 1 person (@~9PM/person/year) while UNEW and MAT will assign 1 person @~5-7PM/person/year. 
 
Deliverables: 
D5.1 Simulation models of the existing biorefineries & KPIs baseline [MAT, M13] Description of the simulation 
model of the baseline biorefining processes, at UPM, FhG and Clariant, including defined KPIs. 
D5.2 Simulation model of improved biorefinery concepts (PI technologies implemented) & report on KPIs [MAT, 
M33]. Description of the simulation model of improved biorefinery processes including KPI analysis.

D5.4 Pilot and validation report of PI methods to process hardwoods of HMC from paper mill sources [UPM,

D5.5 Pilot and validation report of PI methods to process hardwoods of HMC from Organosolv biorefineries [FhG,

process parameters on the laboratory scale

Objectives:  
• To assess and maximise the sustainability impacts of the BioSPRINT processes and products through provision 
of objective information regarding all important sustainability aspects using scientific, transparent and 
reproducible methodologies. 
• To assess the sustainability and techno -economic feasibility of the BioSPRINT processes and products along 
their entire life cycle in a streamlined and comprehensive manner. 
• To provide an iterative feedback to the process developers  (WP1-4) in the form of preliminary LCA/LCC and 
TEA results, which will be used to further optimise the processes in the course of the project. 
 
Task 6.1 – Definitions, settings and system description (DEC, IFEU, MAT). T6.1 provide the goal and scope of 
the ILCSA as well as all initial definitions, settings and system description, including technological parameters. 
ST6.1.1 Definitions and settings for the sustainability assessment: IFEU will provide the goal and scope definitions 
and settings for the ILCSA assessment, which will be used by T6.2 to T6.6 to guarantee a coherent analysis.  Key 
questions to be answered by the assessment will be defined. The scope definition covers a description of the 
investigated system, defines the system boundaries, functional unit s, etc. The settings for the life cycle inventory 
(LCI) analysis will define the technical reference, geographical and time -related coverage and the co -product 
handling. The results will be discussed and agreed upon at an internal project workshop in conjun ction with a 
progress meeting to ensure the optimal execution of the ILCSA. 
ST6.1.2 Qualitative system description: DEC, supported by MAT, will produce qualitative process flow diagrams 
(PFDs) for the BioSPRINT value chains, as well as for all relevant ref erence systems. Thereby, a qualitative 
description of the entire BioSPRINT concept will be provided. The system description will be continuously updated.   
ST6.1.3 Quantitative system description: MAT, supported by DEC, will compile mass and energy balance s for all 
BioSPRINT products as well as for all reference systems identified above. For this purpose, the process models 
developed in WP5 will be used. These data sets will form the foundation for all elements of the ILCSA (WP6). 
Task 6. 2 – Environmental Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) (IFEU). This task will evaluate the environmental 
impacts associated with the targeted BioSPRINT products in comparison to all selected reference products  and 
address the environmental aspects and potential environmental impacts (e.g. use of resources, environmental 
consequences of emissions) of the products/processes. A wide range of impact categories will be covered (e.g. 
climate change, ozone depletion, acidification, eutrophication and resource depletion), providing a comprehe nsive 
picture of the environmental implications. The assessment includes the product’s entire life cycle from raw material 
acquisition through production, use, end-of life treatment, recycling and final disposal. Screening LCAs following 
the guidelines of ISO 14040/14044 on product life cycle assessment will be carried out. Moreover, EN 16760 (Bio-
based products – LCA) as well as the ILCD handbook will be considered. This task uses the mass and energy 
balances compiled in T6.1.3 and provides input for T6.6 (quantitative indicators for key environmental aspects such 
as energy and GHG emission savings). 
Task 6.3 – Techno-economic assessment (MAT, EBOS, UPM). Economic models of the improved biorefinery, 
with PI technologies implemented, will be developed by MAT with the Aspen Process Economic Analyzer. The 
economic models will be linked to the simulation models developed in T5.1.3, for interactive equipment sizing and 
estimation of capital and operating costs (ST6.3.1). This integrated model approach will enable a fast and a detailed 
economic assessment of different configuration of the improved biorefinery process es, particularly i mportant for 
T5.1. The results of the economic model will also be used in ST6.3. 2 to assess the financial performance of the 
improved biorefinery project at different production capacities, in particular to calculate return on investment (ROI) 
or payback period or minimum selling price of new products made from the waste streams, etc. This task will provide 
input for T6.6 (quantitative indicators for key economic aspects such as ROI, payback period and NPV). EBOS will 
contribute to the task with modelling techniques and tools, leveraging advanced data analytics and data visualisation 
techniques. DEC will ensure that the results of the tasks feed into WP1-4 as well as WPs5 and 7. UPM will provide 
relevant economic data and other technical information related to the operations of its biorefining plants. 
ST6.3.1 Capital cost estimate of improved biorefinery (PI implemented ) [MAT]: ST6.3.1 focuses on the 
development of biorefineries’ economic models (new and baseline), which will be linked to simulation models for 
interactive capital cost estimate and the evaluation of operating costs. These will interactively calculate plant's 
CAPEX/OPEX at different production scales, varied operating parameters, different process configurations, etc.

ST6.3.2 Economic and financial assessment of improved biorefinery project [MAT]: ST6.3.2 performs a detailed 
analysis of the economic and financial performance of the improved biorefinery project. For the selected biorefinery 
configuration ROI or payback period of a new project will be calculated. Furthermore, minimum selling price of the 
new products made from HMC streams will be calculated and benched marked against their fossil-based equivalents 
which they intend to replace.   
ST6.3.3 Technological assessment [MAT]: Technological performance and possible risks associated with all 
systems identified in WP5 will be analysed against parameters such as efficiency, state of development, scalability 
issues or process safety risks (investigated in T6.6 and considered here). This will result in a complete description 
of all technological aspects and deliver semi-quantitative and qualitative indicators to the tasks sustainability 
Task 6. 4 – Socio-economic assessment (MAT, IFEU). Socio-economic impact will be assessed by measuring 
BioSPRINT’s contribution to economic and social well-being. T6.4 will assess whether there are any direct risks to 
society e.g. health, safety, economic and environmental security, posed by the implementa tion and uptake of the 
project and target products. Economic well-being indicators will be quantified by estimating: a. Job creation: number 
of jobs which could potentially be created in Europe by BioSPRINT; b. Value creation - monetary value which 
BioSPRINT could create through different revenue streams such as technology development, licensing, engineering, 
product sales, export, etc.  Where applicable social well -being will be qualified by assessing the impact of 
BioSPRINT on: a. Health b. Quality of life  c. Security. BioSPRINT stakeholders e.g. potential technology users, 
product end-users, will be engaged by means of surveys / interviews to provide their input and views on the above. 
Task 6.5 – Process and product safety assessment (UOULU, AEP, MAT)  
ST6.5.1 Process safety, including assessment of technical barriers [UOULU]: The inherent safety of the intensified 
process will be analysed by UOULU. The starting point of the analysis are the chemical compounds, process designs, 
and process conditions ide ntified in the former WPs. Relevant and up -to-date process safety legislation and 
directives will be used as basis for the risk assessments. In addition, ISO 45001 (Occupational health and safety) 
gives overall guidelines and EN ISO 12100 (Safety of machinery - General principles for design - Risk assessment 
and risk reduction) will be utili sed while evaluating the machinery used in the process. The analysis will utili se 
quantitative and qualitative risk assessment techniques such as fault tree analysis, ma chinery safety criteria and 
Hazop (hazard and operability study) to identify potential process safety risks and occupational accident and health 
hazards. Existing risk classification models will be used to define identified risks and their impacts. T6.5 will 
produce a list of identified safety hazards and an analysis focusing on their risk assessment. 
ST6.5.2 Product safety including regulatory (e.g. REACH) compliance assessment [AEP]: AEP will assess the safety 
of new bio-based products, taking into account also their production processes. The use of less hazardous reagents 
and/or solvents (e.g. less volatile, more environmentally friendly, having lighter labelling) will be considered and, 
for each optimised production process, a safety protocol will be prepared, providing the operators with a list of 
instructions and personal protective equipment (PPE). A toxicity prediction of the optimised novolacs and polyols 
will be carried out using up -to-date software based on (Quantitative) Structure -Activity Relationship ([Q]SAR) 
models (e.g. ProTox-II, Osiris). Finally, to remove a severe barrier to commercial exploitation of results, AEP will 
check the REACH status of the new bio-based building blocks (resins, polyols), using HPLC-GPC techniques. If 
the given building block isn’t be compliant to the definition of “polymer”, the synthetic protocol will be modified 
to obtain a new version which – as a “polymer” - is exempted from registration. 
 
Task 6.6 – Integrated life cycle sustainability assessment (IFEU, DEC, MAT). In T6.6, the results from the 
previous analyses ( T6.2-6.5) are integrated to identify and depict the most sustainable pathways among the 
BioSPRINT value chains compared to all reference systems. IFEU will use a multi-criteria evaluation software tool 
following the innovative methodology of ILCSA covering all sustainability indicators chosen. From these analyses, 
the most sustainable pathways will be depicted as well as all optimisation pote ntials. As one central element of 
ILCSA, potential barriers against and unintended side -effects of the implementation of those scenarios that have 
been identified to be in principle sustainable will be analysed. This will lead to conclusions and recommenda tions 
on strategies for a sustainable implementation of the targeted BioSPRINT products. 
Role of the main partners: IFEU leads WP6, overseeing the LCSA, supported primarily by MAT. Justification of 
resources: Over 46 months, IFEU and MAT will assign 1 person @ 5 & 7PM/person/year respectively) 
 
Deliverables:  
D6.1, D6.2 Initial / Final report on definitions, settings and system description for the ILCSA [DEC, M9, M33]          
The reports define the initial / final methodological framework for the BioSPRINT ILCSA.                                                                                                                                
D6.3 Life cycle assessment of HMC-based products [IFEU, M45]. The deliverable provides the final results of the 
environmental assessment carried out at T6.2.    
D6.4 Technological assessment of HMC-based products [MAT, M45]. It includes capital cost estimate, process 
economics of improved biorefinery concepts and a detailed analysis of their economic & financial performance.    
D6.5 Socio-economic assessment of HMC-based products [MAT, M45]. It provides the final results of T6.4.                                                                                                                          
D6.6 Integrated sustainability assessment of hemicellulose -based products [UOULU, M4 3]. The deliverable 
provides the final results of the product & process safety assessment carried out at T6.5.                                                                                                                                                  
D6.7 Integrated sustainability assessment of HMC-based products [IFEU, M46]. The overall and final results of 
the project’s ILCSA.
